Water Availability
20.       The water requirements of the proposed.Mae Klong Irrigation Improve-
ment Project are shown on page 13.  Preliminary hydrologic and operational
studies show that sufficient water will be available for irrigation of the
whole Mae Klong aSea at the projected cropping intensity of 163%, as well.
as providing 50 m /sec for salinity control.and navigation downstream of.,
the Vajiralongkorn Barrage. Annex 17 also shows that there is no con-
straint on water availability during the construction and filling of the
Quae Yai reservoir as development of the Stage II area would probably not
begin until 1983. However, to reconcile power and irrigation requirements
at full irrigation development the 360 MW power station, would have to
onerate at a plant factor of only 15% during the wet season. The plant
factor could be increased to 25% if the marginal East Malaiman development
were not proceeded withi. Alternatively, if pumped storage is provided for
the second stage development of Quae Yai, there would be no problem since
the downstream ptmp reservoir could discharge for irrigation needs a flow
independent of the turbine discharge.

Page 7
Feasibility Study
21.       In order to obtain the necessary data and analysis to form an
adequate basis for appraising the Mae Klong Irrigation Improvement Project,
a feasibility study is required. This study should also examine flood
protection needs, review past and present planning for the whole Mae Klong
Plain and recommend an order of priority for future investments in the
Stage I and Stage II areas. The study would be conducted by the Office
of Irrigated Agriculture, an agency within the Ministry of Agriculture newly
formed to implement to Chao Phya Irrigation Improvement Project (Credit
379 TH) being financed by IDA. Some consultant assistance in advising the
Office and in reviewing the findings of the study would be provided from
the consulting services being financed under Credit 379 TH. It is expected
that the study would take about one year to complete.

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
INTERNATIONAL BOARD OF CONSULTANTS
GEOLOGICAL INVESTIGATIONS OF THE QUAE YAI PROJECT
FOR RESERVOIR LEAKAGE, AN ADEQUATE GROUT CURTAIN
FOR THE DAM, AND AN OPTIIIJM LAYOUT FOR DAM STRUCTURES
1.        The present site of the dam for the Quae Yai project (sometimes
called Quae Yai No. 1) has been studied by the Electric Power Development
Co. (EPDC) of Japan since about 1968 acting as consultants to the Electricity
Generating Authority of Thailand (EGAT). Several reports were prepared
by EPDC and a Feasibility Report became available to the Bank in 1970.
2.        Using this report as a guide, a pre-appraisal mission visited
Thailand in December 1970 and discussed the project with EGAT and EPDC
personnel. The report was not convincing as to the phvsical feasibility
of the project due to the extraordinary large water losses in drill holes
on the site, and the appearance of the foundation rock in several adits
which showed quartzite, limey quartzite and limestone tightly folded and
fractured with deep weathering, clay filled openings, and some small caves.
None of the adits reach groundwater, another indication of highly pervious
abutments. Furthermore, karstic (soluble) limestone was known to exist in
the vicinity of the proposed reservoir and there was little information
available to assure that the reservoir would not have excessive leakage
at locations remote from the dam. No other dam site in the vicinity pro-
mised any better conditions.
3.        Further explorations were done by EGAT and EPDC to resolve the
questions and the Bank representatives suggested and EGAT agreed to appoint
a consulting board to guide these activities which were estimated to take
about one and one-half years. These activities consisted of further drilling
and grout testing at the dam site, and further explorations over the
reservoir area, including the determination of ground water levels in tlle
divides between other drainage areas. If these levels were higher than the
proposed reservoir level, water tightness would be assured.
4.        A three man consulting board was selected (sometimes called the
International Board of Consultants) consisting of a geologist from the UK,
a grouting expert from France, and a rockfill dam expert from the US. It
held its first meeting in July 1971 and visited the dam site, flew over the
reservoir and carried on discussions with EGAT, EPDC and Bank personnel.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
5.        The board was encouraged by the additional investigations carried
out since December 1970. They felt that the right abutment presented
no serious grouting problems because of fairly good rock. However,
groundwater so far as was known, was practically at river level on both
abutments and, therefore, reservoir leakage around properly grouted abutments
might still be a problem. They felt the quality of the left abutment was
so poor that a definite solution was not yet possible either as to feasibility
or costs. Aerial surveys of the reservoir area indicated, but not conclu-
sively, that karstic limestone was not present at critical locations.
Ground surveys indicated some fairly high groundwater levels, but they
could not be considered conclusive. The board geologist outlined a minimum
program of drilling to establish groundwater levels; even a small program
is difficult in this rough jungle area far from a water supply. The rockfill
dam expert suggested a shift in the left abutment of the embankment in the
hope of having to strip less undesirable foundation material; he favored
stripping a core trench rather than trying to grout some of the left abut-
ment material. The grout expert considered the grouting of the right abut-
ment simple, but suggested for the left abutment two lines of holes: one
to be grouted with a mixture of clay,2bentonite, sodium silicate and some
cement under pressures up to 40 kg/cm , the other to be grouted with cement
only at much lower pressures. The board in general did not like the pro-
posed tunnel spillway even though it lowered costs, because of the serious
destruction of tunnel linings in some other similar spillways. The layout
of structures on the right bank which had been criticized as risky at the
first meeting was considered satisfactory by one consultant but EPDC itself
proposed a safer layout.
6.        The second meeting of the Board was held in Thailand in April 1972.
Again a visit was made to the dam site and discussions were held as before.
It was concluded that the investigations made since the first board meeting
had established beyond a reasonable doubt that the reservoir will not leak
excessively. The holes drilled establish the fact that groundwater rises
to or above reservoir elevation at all critical locations. Explorations
disclosed year round flowing streams at many locations and at the upstream
end of the reservoir the head was negligible compared to flow distances for
any leakage.
7.        EPDC had drilled and water tested a number of holes along the
center line of the dam and had reached depths where water losses were
negligible, thus establishing the depth of the grout curtain which would
not be excessive. Drilling on the left abutment will be done from tunnels
to limit depths of grout holes and to permit regrouting if necessary. It
will be done from the surface on the right abutment.  Consolidation area
grouting will be done inder the core to prevent water flows against the
core.
8.        The grouting consultant recommended a grouting test to establish
grout mixtures, pressures, etc. After some discussion it was decided to



ANNEX 2
Page 3
make the test before a civil works contract was signed. EPDC did not con-
sider this necessary but for Bank purposes it would help to assure an
adequate cost estimate. This test and a few holes to establish the end of
the grout curtain on the left abutment were all that remained to be done
in the way of geological explorations.
9.        The proposal made at the first meeting to shift the left end of
the dam upstream was finally rejected as probably more expensive in view
of probable unfavorable geology.
1l.       After the first meeting one board member had suggested that a
cofferdam of reasonable heiglt could be built straight across the river
since the reservoir is so large it can store the spillway design flood
except for the flows through the diversion tunnels. This solution was
accepted after EGAT gave assurances that the land purchasing and resettle-
ment that would be needed at an earlier date with this scheme could be
managed. The very large reservoir also reduced the risk of using the
short flow records which are available.
11.       Responding to the board's objection to the tunnel spillway EPDC
at this meeting proposed an open chute spillway, a better if more expensive
solution. A compromise arrangement of other structures on the right abut-
ment was also presented.






ANNEX 3
Page 1
THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
APPRAISAL QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
ENERGY RESOURCES
1.        The National Energy Authority (NEA) has initiated a study to pre-
pare a long range program for the supply, production and use of energy.
This study will serve as a guide in the development of the energy sector.12
In the period 1956-1970 j2tal energy consumption increased from 10.6 x 10
kcal (1956) to 57.7 x 10   kcal (1970), a 5.5 fold increase or an average
annual growth rate of 13%.
Government Policy
2.        Because of the importance attached to energy and fuel on the
development of the country, the policies enforced are to:
(a) ensure the production and supply of sufficient energy
to meet the needs of the contry;
(b) regulate the price of energy within reasonable ranges;
(c) promote the use of locally available sources of energy;
(d) ensure the most efficient and economical means of
production, transmission and utilization of energy; and
(e) promote domestic industries which change raw materials
into useable fuels (oil refineries).
3.        Since Thailand presently relies mainly on energy derived from
imnorted fuels, the government gives particular attention to the control
of production (oil refineries) to meet the market demand in the most eco-
nomical way. In 1971 Thailand imported crude oil with a foreign exchange
value of about US$190 million equivalent. In the same year, energy
consumption per capita in Thailand amounted to about 220 kg coal equivalent
--about 1/20th of the energy consumption of European countries. Thus with
the ever expanding energy needs, particular emphasis is placed on the
planning of energy development.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
Thailand's Energy Resources
4.        Thailand's energy resources consist of hydro-power, petroleum
(limited but prospecting in the Gulf of Siam is underway), coal, oil shale,
charcoal, wood, paddy husk, and bagasse. Charcoal has been used extensively
for househiold purposes; and wood and paddy husk by industries to produce
process steam. Because of limited quantities of paddy husk and increased
industrial requirements, this fuel source has lost its importance.  Wood
is still an import fuel source, but the country's forests have been gradually
depleted.
Hydra-power
5.        Amongst all types of energy, hydro-power is the most important.
Thailand's geographic situation is favorable with rainfall varying from
1000 mm - 3000 mm per annum.  The river basins in the North and West have
large catchment areas but the rivers themselves are not steep. Thus the
best development consists of high dams which provide storage and head and
regulate seasonal run-off variations. The most important rivers are the
Ping; Nan; Pai; Yuam; and Meklong (the latter has two important tributaries,
the Quae Yai and the Quae Noi). In the South and East rainfall is more
abundant but streams and rivers are small, catchment areas are narrow, and
only medium size dams with small storages can be provided. Energy can
therefore be produced only during the rainy season which in this area
benefits from two monsoons. The important rivers are: Pattani, Sai and Lang.
6.        Besides the water resources within Thailand, there are two interna-
tional rivers, the Mekong and Salaween with an exceptional potential for
hydro power.  Thailand's own potential (84 different projects) totals about
11,300 MW and 40,700 GWh per annum.  Of this potential about 520 MW (4.6%)
with an average annual energy production of about 1900 GWh (4.7%) are pre-
sently exploited.
Coal
7.        Coal is found in many parts of Thailand; both in the North and in
the South. Commercial mining is, however, carried out at only 3 mines (Mae
Moh; Lee; and Lampoon). The estimated reserves at Mae Moh are 120 million
tons of which about 45 million tons can be commercially exploited. (The
heating value is about 6,600 BTU/lb (lignite)). The reserves at Lee are
estimated at 30 million tons of which about 20 million tons can be commer-
cially exploited. (The heating value is about 9,000 BTU/lb). The lignite
deposits at Lampoon (Krabi) are estimated at 25 million tons of which about
10 million tons could be commercially mined.
8.        Most of the lignite mined is presently used as fuel for steam
electric plants established near the mines. Lignite at Mae Moh is also
used to produce fertilizer. About 460,000 t/annum are presently being



ANNEX 3
Page 3
mined of which about 400,000 are used for power production and 60,000 t
for fertilizer. At Mae Moh mining costs vary between US 8-9/t. On the
basis of heating values and the cost of transport, imported heavy fuels
(bunker oil costs about US$22,5/t) 1/ are cheaper for power generation at
Bangkok and thus restrict the use of coal for power generation to power
plants located at the mine sites.
Petroleum
9.        The only source of petroleum found in Thailand is at Fang in the
Chiang-Mai province in the North. Proven reserves are about 2.5 million
barrels and 400,000 barrels have been refined at the site for military
purposes. The refinery started operations in 1958.
10.       The Government has awarded concessions to explore for oil in the
Northeast and in the Gulf of Siam to six foreign companies. No exploratory
results are available to date.
11.       Oil refineries presently in operation in Thailand total 4 with
a capability of processing 166,000 barrels/day.
12.       Oil shale has been discovered at Mae Sod and Lee.  The Mae Sod
deposits are estimated at 2500 million tons which could yield 40-60 gallons
of oil/t. The Lee deposits are estimated to total about 15 million tons
with an oil content of only 12-40 gallons/t. The total crude oil that could
be distilled from these two deposits are estimated at about 2700 million
barrels which could meet Thailand's domestic requirements at the current
rate of consumption for up to 50 years.
13.       A by-product of sugarcane refining (29 sugar refineries in the
country), bagasse is used as the main fuel in the sugar refining process
together with heavy oil and wood. About 90% of the bagaase is used as fuel
each year.  The heating value, however, is low (1.9 x 10  kcal/t) thus
restricting its use to sugar refineries (transport would not be economic).
Garbage
14.       The Bangkok province with a population of about 2.5 million pro-
duces about 700,000 t of garbage each year. At the present 20% is utilized
to produce cliemical fertilizer and 80% is placed in sanitary land fills.
NEA has determined that the heating value is about 2,000 kcal/kg (equivalent
to a low grade cool) and that garbage could be used as a fuel. It has
therefore initiated a project -o construct a 22 MW steam electric plant using
garbage as fuel.
1/   Price July 1973.  Faced with the current level of fuel oil prices,
EGAT proposes to convert one 75 MW oil-fired unit at N. Bangkok to
lignite.



ANNEX 3

Charcoal and Wood

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
APPRAISAL OF THE QUAB YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
THIE METROPOLITAN ELECTRICITY AUTHORITY (MEA)
1.        The Metropolitan Electricity Authority (MEA) is a state enterprise
established in 1958 under "The Metropolitan Electricity Authority Act, B. E.
2501" with the objectives of supplving electricity to the provinces of
Bangkok, Thonburi, Nonthaburi and Samut Prakon. MEA is EGAT's largest con-
sumer, purchasing all of its energy requirements from EGAT.
2.        During the five years 1968-1972, the market served by TEA developed
as follows:
Average annual
1968     1972      growth rate in %
Maximum Demand (MW)                 351.1      643.8          16.1 %
Energy Purchases (GWh)            1,858.9   3,679.1          18.8 %
Energy Sales      (GWh)           1,736.6   3,510.3          19.1 %
Losses            (GWh)             122.3      168.8           8.3 %

3.        To serve this demand, PlEA at the end of FY 1972 had in service 34
HT/primary distribution voltage substations with an installed transformer
capacity of 1290 MVA/1720 I4VA.  (The figures indicate "oil natural cooling"/
"'oil forced air cooling"). Thus installed capacity corresponded to 1.4 -
2.1 times of maximum demand. A range of between 1.6 - 1.8 is generally
accepted as a desired upper range which, compared to the actual ratio,
indicates TlEA's ability to supply the demand on its system.
4.        MEA purchases power and eaergy from EGAT at four terminal sub-
stations. They are North Bangkok, supplied from EGAT's 252.5 MW North
Bangkok Thermal Plant; Bangkok Noi; South Bangkok, supplied from EGAT's



ANNEX 4
Page 2
760 MW South Bangkok Thermal Plant; and Bangkapi. 69 kV and 115 kV sub-
transmission lines link the terminal substations to the 12 kV and 24 kV
distribution substations. These lines had a total length of 362 circuit
kilometers at the end of 1972.
5.        MEA's primary distribution system has two voltage levels, 12 kV
and 24 kV. At the end of 1972, 2388 circuit kilometers were in service.
Its secondary distribution system at 220/440 volts has a length of 6342
circuit kilometers.
The Second Power Distribution System Improvement and Fxpansion Project
6.        This project which covered the five years 1967-1971 was executed
satisfactorily. By the end of 1971, 85% of all forseen projects were com-
pleted. Delays which occurred resulted mainly from a shortage of trained
technical and administrative personnel, and problems associated with pro-
curement:  (a) land necessary for substations and (b) placement of equipment
orders.
7.        To meet the increasing power demand within the area presently
served, but also to expand its system into rural areas, MEA has undertaken
execution of its Third Power Distribution System Improvement and Expansion
Project placing special emphasis to system expansion.
The Third Power Distribution System Imrovement and Expansion Project
8.        This project covering the years 1972-1976 places special emphasis
on:
(a) system expansion to ensure adequate supply of its service area;
(b) planning to ensure economic system development; and
(c) cost control.
9.        To achieve these aims the following targets have been set:
(a) by 1976 MEA would be in a position to serve 92% of the
total poiulation (5.6 million) within its service area   2
(3100 km ) and its network would cover an area of 2300 km
or 75% of the service area; and
(b)  by 1976, XEA estimates maximum demand to reach 1340 14W.
The Mission, however, estimates this demand at 1170 MW
which would represent about 87% of MEA's estimate. For
planning purposes MEA's target is satisfactory since delays
will occur as evidenced by the 85% achievement of the "Second
Project".

11.       The Third Project is subdivided into 4 sub-projects.  They are:
(a)  Construction of 12 new substations; the improvement of 21
existing substations; the construction of 6 new terminal
substations (EGAT supply points); and the improvement of
one existing terminal station;
(b) the construction of 230 kV, 115 kV, and 69 kV transmission
lines radially linking all substations which are provided
with multiple feed-in points. The length to be constructed
over the period totals 217 circuit kin;
(c) the expansion and strengthening of existing distribution
facilities and construction of new facilities; and
(d) the construction of a central load dispatching and operating
center.
Investments 1972-1976
12.       Estimated investments for the 1972-1976 Project are about 1800
million Baht or nearly US$90.0 million equivalent.  Investments per incre-
mental kW of demand (1971 = 558 14W; 1976 = 1170 MW or 612 MW) is about
US$150/kW which is not unreasonable given the load density in the urban
areas.

15.       MEA, with the exception of new loans incurred, had only one loan
outstanding - USAID. Its construction program up 'to and including 1971
had been financed solely from internal resources. Its current expansion
program 1972-1976 will about double assets in service corresponding to a
doubling of maximum demand in the same period.  The above summarized finan-
cial statements indicate:
(a) the rate of return on average net fixed assets in
service remains at an acceptable level; and
(b) MEA's Year end cash position remains positive, well
above working capital requirements, and increases over
the period from 68 million Baht to 223 million Baht
(about US$11.0 million).
16.       In the preparation of these forecasts, MEA has assumed that the
cost of purchased power would decline from 28.15 stang/kWh in 1972 to 27.15
stang/kWh in 1976 due to load factor improvement. These costs in US cents/ki4h
are about 1.35 and 1.31 respectively. iMEA estimates average revenue per kWh
to drop from 41.04 stang/kWh in 1972 to 37.52 stang/kWh in 1976. (US cents
1.97/kWh respectively US cents 1.80/kWh). MEA foresees two tariff reduc-
tions; the first in 1973 averaging 0.68 stang/kWh, and the second in 1975
of an equivalent amount. Over the period, these two reductions, taken into
account in the preparation of the statements, lower revenues by about 235
million Baht.
17.       It is of particular interest to note that to meet its distribution
construction and operation requirements, MEA only needs to increase the cost
of purchased power by 46% (1972) dropping to 38% (1976).  This in part         2
reflects M4EA's restricted area of operations - of its service area (3100 km )
only about 40% or 1235 km 2was served in 1972 - and the relative high load
density of about 600 kW/km in 1972 which remains virtually constant over
the period, inspite of a near doubling of the area served. Normally dis-
tribution authorities need to double the cost of purchased power to meet
their construction and operating commitments.
18.       In conclusion it can be assumed that MEA's financial situation -
in spite of its substantial investment program - remains satisfactory over
the period. Average revenue/kWh, compared to utilities in developed countries,
is not excessive indicating a reasonable margin of flexibility with regard
its tariffs, indicating further that any reasonable tariff increase by EGAT
could be absorbed by MEA by foregoing proposed tariff reductions.

TIAILAND
ELECTRICITY GEN4ERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILNAD
APPRAISAL QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
THE PROVINCIAL ELECTRICITY AUTIORITY (PEA)
1.        The Provincial Electricity Authority (PEA) established in 1960 is
authorised to distribute electricity for residential, commercial and indus-
trial consumption in all provinces of Thailand with the exception of the
Bangkok Metropolitan Area served by the Metropolitan Electricity Authority.
It is under the control of the 14Iinistry of the Interior. PEA purchases
power and energy in bulk from EGAT, but also generates electricity in local-
ities that cannot yet be economically served by EGAT's transmission system.
2.        To meet its increasing demand PEA has continually expanded its
system and presently has in service about 370 diesel generating plants all
over Thailand with an installed capacity of about 75 MW producing about 12%
of its total energy requirements.
3.        The principal statistics covering PEA's services 1968-1972 are
summarized as follows:

5.        In 1971 PEA's investments totalled 128.1 million Baht (US$6.2
million equivalent) of which about 58.7% was contributed from internal cash
generation; 27.4% was provided from the Government's budget; and the balance
of 13.9% by foreign and domestic loans. The respective figures for 1972
were: Total investments 169.4 million Baht (US$8.1 million equivalent) of
which 67% was provided through internal cash generation; 3% from the Govern-
ment's budget and 30% through foreign and domestic loans.  The investments
made in 1971 and 1972 represent a cost/kW of incremental demand of about
US$147. This is not unreasonable, because the large area served by PEA
with its low load density tending to increase costs is offset by the relative
low cost construction.
6.        In 1971 PEA's revenues from the sale of electricity totalled
622.5 million Baht or 67.3 stang/kWh on average (US cents 3.24). In 1972
the respective figures were 762.5 million Baht and 62.9 stang/kWh (US cents
3.02) indicating a tariff reduction effective in this year. Overall PEA
has been in a position to reduce tariffs consistently -- a total of 11 tariff
reductions since its establishment in 1960. It is of interest to note that
PEA's average revenue/kWh sold in 1972 was about 153% of MEA's average
revenue. Efforts are being made towards a uniform tariff system (PEA and MEA)
and EGAT already supplies energy to both authorities under the same tariff.
7.        At present PEA has two main rate schedules:
Rate A: applicable to areas supplied by EGAT: and
Rate B: applicable to areas where PEA generates electricity.
Rate A is divided into 9 schedules: General Regular Service: Small Business
Service; General Business Service; Medium Business Service; Large Business
Service; Water Supply Service; Mining Industry Service; Agricultural Pumping
Service; and Temporary Service. The Block tariff system is utilized whereby
the cost per kWh falls with increased consumption. The first two schedules



ANNEX 5
Page 3
are a direct kWh charge whilst the General Business through Large Business
services also include a demand charge. The Water Supply Service is provided
at a flat rate of Baht 0.60/kWh; the Mining Industry Service and Agricultural
Pumping Service at Baht 0.40/kWh. For Temporary Service a flat rate of
Baht 0.05 - 0.08/Watt/night is charged.
Rate B is divided into 7 schedules: General Regular Service: Small Business
Service; General Business Service; Water Supply Service; Mining Industry
Service; Agricultural Pumpting Service; and Temporary Service. The Block
tariff system is also utilized here.  The first two schedules are a direct
kWh charge whilst the General Business Service also has a demand charge.
Water Supply Service is provided at a flat rate of Baht 0.70/kWh; the MSining
Industry is charged at a rate of Baht 0.50/kWh; and the Agricultural Pumping
Service is charged Baht 0.60/kWh. Temporary Service is charged at the same
rate as in the main Schedule A.
8.        A domestic consumer utilizing 250 kWh/month would pay under Rate
A Baht 257.20/month or about Baht 1.03/kWh (US$12.4; respectively US cents
4.94/kWh). Under Rate B the same consumer would pay Baht 341.60/month or
about Baht 1.37/kWh (US$16.42; respectively US cents 6.56/kWh).
The Second National Economic and Social Developnent Plan 1967-1971
9.        Under this plan PEA carried out 9 distinct projects.  Of these
3 were completed in 1969 with an investment of about 261 million Baht.
They covered the Yanhee First Stage, Nam Pong and the Riural Electrification
Project (Phase 1.)  The investments in the other 6 projects of  hlich 4 were
completed in 1972 totalled about 545 million Baht. The completed projects
covered the Yanhee Second Stage; Krabi; Mae Hong Son; and the Second Phase
of the Rural Electrification Project. The Lam Dom Noi and Lower Southern
Projects (total investments 167 million Baht) are scheduled for completion
in 1974 and 1975 respectively.
10.       As in the case of MEA, project implementation has not been carried
out on schedule due to the lack of experienced technical and administrative
personnel. PEA operates -- besides the Head Office in Bangkok -- 697 offices
in the Provinces. Its staff in 1972 totalled 6327 of which 1569 were
employed in Bangkok and 4758 in the Regions. The staff/consumer ratio is
a very favorable 1/90 indicating a serious staff shortage in view of its
widespread operations.
The Third National Economic and Social Development Plan 1972-1976
11.       To meet the increasing demand for power and einergy and to
expand its system into areas not presently served, PEA has formulated a
development program covering the years 1972-1976. This program consists
of:



ANNEX 5
Page 4
(a)  projects in the area serviced with EGAT power consisiting
of 4 sub-projects with investments totalling about 108
million Baht of which about 51 million Baht would represent
foreign exchange expenditures;
(b)  ptrojects in the area served by PEA diesel plants consisting
of 6 sub-projects with investments totalling about 259
million Baht of which about 128 million Baht would represent
foreign exchange expenditures;
(c)  projects to extend primary distribution lines to centers
presently supplied by diesel generators. The number of
centers affected are 93 and investments are estimated at
about 190 million Baht of which about 72 million Baht
would represent foreign exchange expenditures;
(d)  Accelerated Rural Electrification.  The feasibility of
this project is under review and PEA is being assisted
by a team from USAID. Estimated investments total about
450 million Baht over a five year period of which about
154 million Baht would represent foreign exchange costs.
The objectives are to electrify about 500 villages per
year;
(e)  expansion of existing systems to meet increased 4emand.
Investments for this project total about 345 million Baht
with an estimated foreign exchange content of about 223
million Baht; and
(f)  a project to provide 30 villages/year (1973-1976) withi small
diesel generating units and distribution systems. A first
stage of such a project was completed in 1972. Annual
investments are estimated at about 5.4 million Baht/year
provided from the Government's budget.

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
TRAINING
INTRODUCTION
1.dl      The three government owned electricity supply authorities EGAT,
MEA and PEA have jointly, with the National Energy Authority (NEA) and
Electricite de France (EdF), established an Electrician Training Center
to train technicians, foremen, and skilled workmen. In accordance with
their individual interests and responsibilities EGAT, MEA, PEA and NEA
conduct their own courses. Other private enterprises or other government
offices who are interested in the courses offered can send their personnel
to the center.
1.02      Other categories of personnel such as executives, engineers and
adninistrative personnel are sent to local educational institutes and abroad
to gain experience.
OBJECTIVES
1.03      The objectives are:
(a) to provide for refresher courses;
(b) to train new personnel;
(c) to facilitate the transfer of technical employees within
the respective authority;
(d) to qualify the capable technicians, foremen and skilled
workmen; and
(e) to improve safety.
TRAINING COURSES
1.04      The number of trainees passing through the training center averaged
over the past five years about 225/year. The courses offered are: improve-
ment courses for skilled workmen and technicians in power distribution
technology, electromechanics, meters and relays; training courses for



ANNEX 6
Page 2
metering, instrumentation, protection and control room equipment, electro-
mechanics, and interior wiring; and specialized courses for skilled workers
and technicians for wiring, air-conditioning equipment, and automobile
electrics.
1.05      Short improvement courses are divided equally into classroom study
(theory) and practical application. The training courses consist of (a) 15
weeks basic training (common to all levels); (b) 5 weeks initiation of
fundamentals; (c) a specialized course of 24 weeks duration (meters, pro-
tection, control room equipment, electromechanics) or 16 weeks duration
(interior wiring); and various specialized courses are given varying from
60 - 90 hours duration.
EGAT
EGAT relies on on-the-job training and for senior staff provides
training possibilities abroad. Its use of the Technical Training Center
can be summarized as follows:
1968           Ill employees
1969           102 employees
1970           120 employees
1971            59 employees
1972            50 employees
MEA
MEA presently employs about 7,000 personnel. Parallel with MEA's
past load growth (on average about 20% p.a.) new staff will need to be con-
tinually recruited. Thus MEA's objectives are to:
(a) increase working efficiency;
(b) prepare personnel for promotion;
(c)  develop top and middle management;
(d) improve methodology; and
(e) improve working and public relations.
MEA's training program covers top management development courses; courses
for technicians, linemen and servicemen; and ih-service training for engineers
and administrative personnel. The number of MEA employees trained at the
Training Center totalled:



ANNEX 6
Page 3
1968           41
1969            80
1970            91
1971          105
1972            27
PEA
PEA trains about 50 employees per year.  The training provided at
the Center is similar to that available from other established vocational
schools with the addition, however, of special technical courses commensurate
with the responsibilities of staff. The training duration is normally three
years with persons in possession of a high school certificate.  Training is
divided into theoretical courses and on-the-job training.  The number of
PEA employees completing courses at the Training Center numbered:
1968            57
1969           51
1970           73
1971            52
1972            24

TRANSMISSION

55 serve PREA and 6 EGAT's industrial consumers. MEA is served at 69 kV and PEA
at 11 kV, 22 kV and 33 kV. As of November 30, 1972 EGAT's transmission facilities

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
PROJECT DESCRIPTION
GENERAL
1.        The Quae Yai River originates in the mountains forming the border
between Thailand and Burma and flows south through the mountain region
generally following the predominant geological structures. On the east, the
Quae Yai River Basin bounds on the Chao Phya Basin and on the west, the
basin of the Quae Noi River which joins the Quae Yai near Kanchanaburi forming
the Mae Klong River which flows into the Gulf of Siam.
2.        The Quae Yai River is about 380 m long and the catchment area
above the junction of the Quae Noi is about 14,800 sq km. In its upper
reaches the Quae Yai valley is steep and generally narrow. Immediately
downstream of the junction with the Huai Kha Khaeng, its largest tributary,
the Quae Yai River enters a wide and open valley. Before reaching the
western end of the Central Plain, the Quae Yai once more passes through
a narrow gorge about 80 km upstream of Kanchanaburi. This gorge forms a
favorable dam site for a huge reservoir and the Project is located in this
area.
THE DAM
3.        The darm to be built will be a rock-fill structure with a center
impervious core, about 140 m in height and with a crest length of about
615 m. The crest elevation is 185 m, affording with a normal full supply
level of 178.5 m a freeboard of about 6.5 m.  The3total embankment of the
dam, has an estimated volume of about 13 million m .
4.        The project site has a favorable topography permitting the con-
struction of a common forebay for the intakes and the spillway by excavation
in a saddle on the right bank. Materials excavated can be used for the
daw embanikment.
5.        According to geological investigations (see Annexes 2 and 18) the
foundation rock at the damsite is heavily fissured due to folding and
weatlhered to a considerable depth. Large faults have not been located.
For a concrete dam, the foundations would not be stable enough. However,



ANNEX 12
Page 2
a rock-fill dam -- the base of which covers a wide area -- does not need
the bearing strength required for a concrete dam and can be safely built
provided that adequate precautions are taken (see also Annex 18). All
aggregates required for the construction of a concrete dam would have to
be quarried and crushed because of the absence of river deposits (sand and
gravel) in adequate quantities. For a rock-fill dam, however, the materials
required are abundant and readily available in the vicinity of the dam.
6.        A rock-fill dam was chosen because the volume of impervious core
required is small with regard total volume. The rock-fill zone will be
embanked with sound and high shear strength rock which will reduce the
cross-section. The adoption of a relatively thin impervious tore will also
reduce the volume of excavation. A thin earth core also has the advantage
of quickly dispersing pore pressure developing during construction and thus
facilitating the implacement of core materials which is influenced by weather
conditions.
7.        The impervious core inclines slightly and has filter zones on the
upstream and downstream sides. The transition zone, to be provided between
thte filter zone and the rock-fill zone on the downstream side, alleviates
abrupt changes in the grading of the embankment materials suppotting the
core. Rock will be placed in such a manner to have materials of larger size
on the outer side and smaller size on the innet side. Rip-rap will be
placed on the upstream surface to prevent erosion by wave action.
SPILLWAY
8.        The spillway is an open-channel chute type.  Five radial gates will
be installed to control the discharge. The maximum spillway capacity has
been determined together with the determination of the maximum ptobable
flood. (See Annex 16).
INTAKE STRUCTURE
9.        Built adjacent to the spillway, the intake will be designed in such
a manner that water can be drawn from the reservoir down to elevation 147 m.
Each intake will be provided with roller gates oterated by a gantry crane.
The intake structure for 5 inlets will be completed in the first phase
(3 x 120 MW = 360 MW first phase; followed by 2 x 180 MW = 360 MW reversible
pump-turbine units in a second phase). The inlets of future units will be
closed by temporary bulkheads.
PENSTOCKS
10.       All welded penstocks about 240 m in length will conduet the water
from the intakes to the turbines. The inner diameter decreases from 5.5 m
at the upper end to 4.5 m at the lower end.  Design flow is 120 m3/sec for
each penstock.



ANNEX 12
Page 3
POWERHOUSE A4D SWITCHYARD
11.       The powerhouse to house 3 units consisting of the main machine
room, control room, repair workshop, stores and station service unit is a
conventional reinforced concrete structure about 38 m high; 28 m wide at
the top and 50 m wide at the botton; and 80 m long. Excavation will be
carried out for all 5 units to avoid blasting when the first three units
are operational. The excavated area for units 4 and 5 will be backfilled
witll loose material.
12.       The switchyard is located on the opposite bank to the power house.
Sufficient space is available for 5 units.
GENERATING EQUIPI4ENT
13.       A high voltage synchronizing unit system is foreseen because of
its high dependability and operating freedom. The turbine and generator
control system will be centrally operated fron the control room. The
turbines are vertical shaft Francis units each of about 125 MW cipacity
operating at 167 rpm and with a discharge of not less than 120 m /sec at
rated head. Further optimisation studies are necessary to determine the
most economic turbine characteristics.
14.       The generators will be vertical shaft, umbrella type, synchronous
with damper windings and air-coolers, installed in a concrete barrel. Unit
capacity is about 140 tIVA and the frequency is 50 cycles.
15.       The transformers will be outdoor, three-phase, forced oil and
forced air-cooled type each of 140 MVA capacity. They will be installed on
the tailrace side of the powerhouse.
16.       For station service, a horizontal shaft Francis unit (3 MW) will
be installed in the powerhouse.
17.       For unloading, assembly and disassembly of the turbines, generators,
transformers etc. two 200-t overhead cranes will be installed.
18.       The bus-lines between the generators and transformers will be of
the isolated phase enclosed bus type and disconnecting switches, potential
transformers, current transformers, etc. to be connected with the bus-lines
will be enclosed in cubicles to ensure security and to minimize space.
TRANSMISSION
19.       To transmit the 360 1W station output to Bangkok, two 230 kV
transmission circuits will be required. The transmission line will extend
north from the Bangkok Noi substation turning left towards Quae Yai. After
turning left the line runs parallel with the railroad and highway and then
follows the new access road to the Project.



ANNEX 12
Page 4
20.       Supports to be used will be two-circuit steeltowers with three
conductors suspended on each side. Since the annual frequency of lightning
occurrence along the transmission line route is high, two overhead ground
wires are provided to shield the conductors.
SUBSTATION
21.       Power will be transmitted to the Bangkok Noi substation to the
west of Bangkok. This substation will form part of a 230 kV ring transmis-
sion system encircling Bangkok. The connection system of the two lines
will be of the transfer bus type, identical to the present system. Arrange-
ments of electrical equipment, circuit breakers, disconnecting switches,
current transformers etc, will conform to the existing layout. Additional
panels will be installed in the control room.
COTIMUNICATION EQUIPMENT
22.       Communication equipment will include a multi-channel microwave
radio telephone, power line carrier telephone, and UHF radio. Load dis-
patching will be commanded from the Nlorth Bangkok Dispatch Center. To
facilitate communication during construction, the microwave radio telephone
will be installed early.
CONSTRUCTION SCHEDULE
23.       The construction schedule is outlined in Annex 15.  By July 1973
specifications for the civil contract will be prepared for issuance inee-
diately thereafter. It was anticipated that a contract could be awarded by
January 1974. 1/ During calendar 1974 the principal activity will be concen-
trated to prepare camp facilities and construction roads and to install
construction plant and equipment. Excavation of the two diversion tunnels
will commence in this year. During calendar 1975 excavation of the diver-
sion tunnels will continue and excavation for the coffer dam is scheduled
to be started.  In November 1975 it should be possible to divert the river.
At the same time excavation for the dam will proceed as will excavation for
the spillway, intakes and penstocks. The first fill for the main dam is
expected to be placed in early 1976 and completed in two years (early 1978).
Filling would conmaence in July 1977. In early 1976, excavation for the
powqerhouse would commence and installation of the electromechanical equip-
ment would start in late 1977. Tenders for such equipment would need to be
issued b)v mid 1974 and contracts awarded early 1975.  Transmission and
suibstation facilities would be constructed during the period end 1974 --
mid 1979. Specifications would need to be issued by mid 1974 and contracts
awarded by early 1975. Prior to contract award the final surveys would be
carried otut.  The units are scheduled to become operational in August and
tlovember 1979 and March 1930. The critical date is July 1977 the begin of
filling. If a delay should occur and the flood season 1977 missed, opera-
tions of the scheme would be delayed one year. From mid 1974 to the end
of 1978, resettlement work needs to be carried out whiich is considered ample
in view of the excellent Ecological Report and recommendations already
available.
1/   Current estimate mid-April 1974.

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF TIAILAND
QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
HYDROLOGY
GENERAL
1.        The Quae Yai River originates in the mountains forming the border
between Thailand and Burma and flows south through the mountain region
generally following the structures. On the east the Quae Yai basin bounds
on the Chao Phya Bassin and on the west, the basin of the Quae Noi River
which joiIIs the Quae Yai near Kanchanaburi forming the Mae Klong River
which flows into the Gulf of Siam.
2.        The catchment area at the damsite is about 10830 sq. km.  There
are no existing lhydro-electric developments on the Quae Yai nor on the
Quae Noi. However, at Tha -tMuang, about 10 km downstream of Kanchanaburi, a
headwork diversion dam has been constructed by thie Royal Irrigation Depart-
ment (RID) to supply irrigation water to about 500,000 ha of fertile land
in the western part of the Central Plain.
CLIMATE
3.        The climate of the project area is tropical monsoon.  From November
to February, the area has little rainfall and moderate temperature due to
the NE monsoon bringing cold and dry air from the China mainland. March
and April is the hot season attributable in part to the retreat of the NE
monsoon. From May to September, the SW monsoon prevails, bringing a stream
of warma htmid air from the Indian Ocean, and rainfall is abundant. October
is the transitional period from the SW to the NE monsoon.
4.        The annual mean temperature, monthly maximum temperature, and
monthly minimum temperature at the Project site are 29.0C, 38.0C; and
17C respectively. The highest temperature recorded was 43.7C and the
lowest 0.1C.
5.        The annual rainfall of the Quae Yai basin is about 1600 am in the
upper reaches, about 1100 mm in the basin of the tributary Huai Kha Khaeng,
and about 1000 mm at the Project site. For the catchment area as a whole,
average annual rainfall is about 1300 mm.



ANNEX 16
Page 2
HYDROLOGY
6.        On the Quae Yai River there are 5 streamflow gauging stations.  In
addition there are two on the Quae Noi, one on the liuai Ta Poen River, and
two on the Mae Klong River downstream of the Quae Yai-Quae Noi junction.
The Kong Rieng gauging station on the Quae Yai is about 4 km upstream of
teie damsite. This station has been in operation since 1952.
7.        Available data was verified by comparing simultaneous run-off data
of the three Quae Yai gauging stations (Kang Rieng, Ongkla and Khao Chod).
The three hydrographs are similar and their quantative and time-lag relations
resonable. The Kang Rieng rating curve was made on the basis of an adequate
number of direct measurements using a current meter and revised yearly (upto
3 times/year) to cope with river bed changes. The rating curves are con-
sidered to be reliable. The correlation between annual average run-off
and annual average rainfall within the Kang Rieng catchment area is gocd.
8.        Since only a short period of run-off records is available, 1952-1971,
the correlation between run-off and rainfall is particularly important
because of the longer rainfall records (available since 1911).
9.        The average annual rainfall in Bangkok and Kanchanaburi from 1952-
19('7 was 1530 mm and 1040 mm respectively. For the period 1911-1967 the
corresponding figures are 1440 mm and 950 mm. Based on run-off records at
the Ban Tham gauging station on the IMae Klong below the junction of the
Quae Yai and Quae Noi rivers - for which records are available from 1940-
1966 - a smili5r comparison showed 409 m /sec average run-off for 1952-1966
and also 409 m /sec for the 1940-1966 period. Therefore from the point
of viexw of determining the energy prodluction for the life of the project
the 1952-1971 period is considered adequate, especially since this period
includes a dry period of several years. To determine, however, the filling-
up period of the reservoir the situation is more critical and a special
analysis was carried out described more fully in Annex 17.
River-Run-off
1(.       The run-off was calculated according to usual and acceptable
criteria in that the specific run-off of the various gauging stations was
correlated to the run-off data available at Kang Rieng. A correlation
between average annual rainfall within each sub-catchment area was also
carried out.  The specific run-off of the Quae Yai increases in thle upper
reaches giving ratios of 1.6 for Kao Chod; 1.2 for Ongkla; and 0.63 for
Ban Tham with a value of 1 assigned to Kang Rieng. A similar trend is found
for rainfall.
Flood Flow
11.       Since the proposed dam is a rockfill structure, the determination
of the maximun  probable flood is of particular significance in the design
of the spillway capacity and free board.  Two methods were utilized:
statistical and physical.



ANrIX 16
Page 3
12.       Statistical.  The probability calculation was based on past flood
records at the Kang Rieng gauging station using acceptable methods. The
results obtained can be summarized as follows:
3          Flood Volu9e
Frequency           Peak Flood m /s           million m
20                  2700                    2520
50                  3300                    2820
100                  3700                    3090
200                  4100                    3370
1000                  5000                    3970
5000                  5300                    4500
10000                  6300                    4840
13.       Physical:  This method gives the probable maximum precipitation
that may happen if all factors contributing to the generation of precipita-
tion were to reach their most critical conditions simultaneously. For the
Quae Yai River, 15 historic storms were studied and a period of one month
was determined as the duration of the storms. The results of these studies
were as follows:
Maximum Probable Peak Flood          6000 m3/sec
Maximum Probable Flood Volume        4920 million m
14.       Thus the statistical and physical methods utilized gave nearly the
same results. Nevertheless, a renewed analysis was carried out described
below:
(a) Historic rainfalls were obtained from the storm of 30th
September - 9th October 1963. The probable maximum
precipitation was estimated without modification of the
rainfall pattern according to intensity-duration-frequency
phenomenon, an amount of 5 nmUn continuous loss was then
subtracted to sustain the base flow, and effective
rainfall was assumed at 80%, 75% and 70% to compute
the probable maximum flood by the distribution ratio
method; and
(b) The probable maximum flood was based on the unit hydrograph.
The same rainfall data, loss, base flow and effective
rainfall was used as in (a) above.

16.       This calculation was further checked with the use of the maximizing
factor.  This factor, defined as the ratio of the maximum moisture inflow
index to the historical moisture inflow index, was found to be 1.70 and 1.72
for 96% and 100% relative humidity. (For the Nan River this factor was
1.13).  Using 1.7 and380% effective rainfall, the probable maximum flood
calculated was 7102 m /sec and the flood volume 3870 million M3.
17.       For the purpose of design the maximum probable flood and flood
volume as calculated in the preceding paragraph was adopted. To allow for
increased safety, however, the normal high water level was reduced 1.5 m
to 178.5 m and the spillway crest elevation reduced by 1 m to 171.0 m.   3
Under these conditions maximum reservoir discharge would amount to 2582 m /sec
of -which 2422 m /sec over the spillway and 160 m /sec through the outlets,
and maximum reservoir elevation would amount to 182.4 m i.e. 2.6 m below
tiie dam crest level. The 2.6 m freeb ard could accommodate an additional
flood volume of about3l,000 million m . Thus a maximum flood volume of
about 4,900 million m  could be acconmodated, meeting the requirements as
given in paragraphs 12 and 13.
L,vaporation
13.       Evaporation losses from the reservoir can be estimated by deducting
tiue evapotranspiration of the original ground surface from the evaporation
of the reservoir water surface. Two methods were utilized which gave annual
evaporation losses of 425 mmn and 440 mm. A rounded off figure of 500 mm
was used to determine the annual net possible evaporation loss.
Sedimentation
19.       Sedimentation of the storage was estimated on data collected at
the Ban Tham site. Total sedimentation volume wihich includes suspended
ssdiment load and bed load transportation at Ban Tham is about 4.6 million
m /year for a catchment area of about 25,500 sq. km. This gives 180 m /sq.km.
20.       From this value, total sediment at the proposed damsite (catchment
area 10,880 sq. km.) is about 2 inllion m /year.  This would give a life of
the reservoir of 17,200 million m (Volume dividV by 2) - 8600 years. With
an effective stoSage capacity of 4,350 million m , dead storage amounts to
12,850 million m which provides for over 6,000 years sedimentation. Thus
over the projects estimated life of 1003years, total sediment encroachment
oni the reservoir is about 200 million m , just over 1% of the storage volume.

QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
OPERATIONAL STUDIES
1.        To determine the compatibility of power generation and irrigation
two studies were carried out. The first related to the period of initial
impounding of the Quae Yai Reservoir and examined the effects of phased
irrigation demands from the progressive development of the Stage I and Stage
II Meklong irrigation project (see Annex 1) and power releases on the rate
of filling of the reservoir. The second examined the steady state condition
in which the oDeration of the Quae Yai reservoir was studied under the
ultimate irrigation demands following full development. Both studies covered
the 20 year period of available stream-flow records 1952-1971.
2.        Irrigation demands were established from consumptive use require-
ments of the various crops projected.  The demands at the Vajiralongkorn
diversion barrage were derived from the consumptive use estimates by making
allowance for effective rainfall, farm and canal efficiencies. For the
filling studies effective rainfall was assuined at a 3/4 low level (i. e.
a level that would be exceeded in three years out of four). Demands at the
diversion barrage increased from vear to year to reflect the phased introduc-
tion of irrigation development, particularly on-farm development.
3.        It was assumed that the Quae Yai Reservoir would start to fill from
June 1977 and dry season water wqiuld be available from December 1979.  Demands
at the barrage, including a 50 m /sec downstream demand for salinity control,
were translated to requirements from Quae Yai by establishing the incre-
mental requirement over and above available flows in the other tributaries
of the fleklong. For the 2nd, 4th and 6th years of filling it was assumed
that 3/4 low flows would be available from tributaries other than Quae Yai
and for all other years median flows. The resulting requirements are given
on pages 6 and 9 of this annex.
4.        With these requirements and the 20 years of available stream flow
records of the Quae Yai as inflow to the reservoir, 20 filling cycles were
examined, one starting in each year of record in order to establish the time
to reach full operating regime unider a number of probabilities.
5.        For the steady state study, the effects of actual rainfall addi-
tional to the 3/4 low level was examined for each month of each year. Con-
servative assumptions were made on the efficiency of additional rainfall
in reducing stored water demands in view of the time lag between water leaving
Quae Yai and reaching the field - probably 3 days or more.



ANNEX 17
Page   2
6.        Witni the adjustments for effective rainfall, demands at tne diver-
sion barrage were established for each month of each year of the period
1952-1971 and with the allowance for salinity control, translated to require-
iments from the storage oy taking into account the actual flow in tributaries
of the IMeklong river other than Quae Yai during these months. The opera-
tion studv was then run with imatching Quae Yai inflows to determine the
power output of the project under ultimate operational conditions.
7.        The results of the filling runs indicated no conflict between
power and irrigation durinig initial impounding. However, for steady state
operation it was found necessary to reduce the minimum monthly continuous
power from 120 MWl to 90 :4W and to introduce a reducing factor of 60% to
define output wihen operating below the rule curve in order to prevent the
reservoir being evacuated during the simulated 20 years operation. Thus
a plant factor of 15% (equivalent to 3 1/2 hours of operation per day at
full output) would be applicable to the wet season mqonths July through
October for a number of years. This indicates a degree of inflexibility in
the operation of the project for power and a conflict with water demands for
irrigation. Even so, the output of Quae Yai can be readily and effectively
absorbedi by EGAT's system. Due to this a marginal analysis was performed
for the various sub-projects of the Stage II Irrigation area. Two critical
areas were envisaged: (a) the Lower Right Bank area which indicates salinity
problems, and the East Ilalaiman area which consists of a Large portion of
broadcast rice. The results for the Lower Right Bank area indicate that
even in the most severe conditions anticipated, the estimated internal rate
of return is above the assumed opportunity cost of capital (12%).  The
East Malaiman area, however, will deserve additional attention since the
estimated economic internal rate of return equals the opportunity cost of
capital only under the most favorable assumptions.  Thus, should it prove
more economic to reduce the assumed scope of irrigation development, power
prospect would improve.
3.        The only operational concern resulting from the need to operate
the power plant to Tlaximize both irrigation and power benefits results from
the aboverentioned low plant factor operation.  To attain an output of 360
=ft, 360 m /sec would le released during 3 1/2 hours.  The sudden release
of water could affect navigation anid could endanger life.
9.        However, this state would only exist once the irrigation area
is fully developed (1994). By this time the second phase of the Quae Yai
Scihemne would be operational.  This phase which forsees the installation
of two 180 PIN units would also be provided with a downstream re-regulating
pond to serve as a reservoir for pumping water. Releases further downstream
fromi this reservoir xwould be spread over the full day thus providing a
fairly constant river flow downstream eliminating any hazards and improving
navigation conditions.



ANNEX 17
Page   3
ll.       The results of the studies given in subsequent pages of this
annex can be summarized as follows:
(a) Reservoir filling

The results of this study also provided the yearly energy generation and the
weighted average generation for purposes of the economic evaluation of the
power project.
(b) Steady State Operation
The important conclusion to be drawn from this study is
that rated output (360 MW) could be achieved in every
month of the 20 year period. Yearlg average inflow into
the reservoir totals 4347 million m ; ev4poration averages
3 
189 million m ; and spill 17.0 million m . Irrigation
requirements average 3230 million m . There is no shortage
of irrigation water in any month of the 20 year period.
The surface of the reservoir varies between a minirnum of
162.4 m and the maximum of 180.0 m (median minimum level,
168.8 m; and median maximum level 175.4 m confirming the
assumption of initial level made (175.0 m). Average power
for the period is 141.1 MW giving an average plant factor
of 39.4%. Minimum average power is 54 MW (plant factor 15%);
and maximum average power 324 K4 (plant factor 90%). Average
yearly generation is 1230 GWh. Since EGAT's system is a
mixed thermal-hydro system, power available can be readily
and effectively absorbed by the system.
12.       The analysis carried out was also based on the following assumptions:



ANNEX 17
Page 4
(a) rule curves for reservoir operation were established.
(i) upper rule curve. On exceeding this rule curve it
was assumed that power generation would be at full
output;
(ii)  between the rule curves continuous power output
was limited to 90 MW; and
(iii) lower rule curve. When the reservoir drops below
this curve continuous output was reduced to 54 14W
(i.e. 60% of 90 .W)
(b)  turbine efficiencies were established as a function of
available head (varying from 84% at 150.0 m elevation to
92tl at elevations between 165 - 185 m)
(c)  a penstock efficiency of 9L% was assumed corresponding
to a head loss of over 2.3 m;
(d)  a generator efficiency of 97% was assumed;
(e)  evaporation losses were determined as a function of the
storage surface and month;
(f)  energy output is a resultant of flow, head, and machine
efficiencies;
(g)  in determining power output irrigation requirements were
given priority.
13.       The studies carried out, which were based on conservative assump-
tions, indicate the viability of the proposed project and provided essential
date for the economic justification of tne project. Similar studies will
need to be carried out prior to initial impounding to take into account
tlie definite established irrigation requirements and a further five years
of stream records.

ANNEX 18
Page   1
THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTllORITY OF tHAILAND
QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
GEOLOGY
GE.NERAL
1.        The strata in the vicinity of the damsite comprise the so called
Kanchanaburi Series of the Paleozoic era. West of the damsite there are
metamorphic rocks and to the east there are stocks and batholiths of granite.
The Kanchanaburi series can be divided into 3 formations: A, the lower; B,
the middle; and C the top.
2.        Formation A.  These consist of quartzite, calcareous sandstone,
slate, calcareous shale, and impure limestones. The damsite is located in
this formation. Quartzite is widely distributed while slate is localized.
At the damsite there is calcareous sandstone and an intercalation of impure
limestone. This formation has been subjected to severe folding and thin
layers of argillaceous (clay) rock are considerably crushed.
3.        Formation B.  Consisting of alternations of shaly limestone and
shiale, this formation is found in the flat area on the left bank of the
river and has also been subjected to severe folding.
4.        Formation C.  This formation does not exist in the vicinity of
the damsite. It consists of alternations of sandstone, shale and thin
calcareous facies.
5.        Rat Buri limestone is widely distributed in the area west of the
dairisite and a karstic topography is presented, but limited to an area of
puire Rat Buri limestone and not found in the area of irmpure limestone in
the Kanchanaburi Series.  The axis of folding of the latter series in gen-
erally NWM-SE with the respective strikes of the three formations in the
same direction coinciding with the topography of the Quae Yai River Basin.
n.        The characteristic features are that the rocks of each foriation
are multifarious, but calcareous rocks predominate and there are various
thiickness of limestone with large amounts of impurities.  Prominent faults
are not seen at the dansite.  Strikes and dips of the bedding plane of the
formiations at the damsite are not constant, but as a whole the strikes are
roughly parallel to the river while the dips are either NE or SW. The
foundation rock of the dansite is more severely folded on the left bank
than the riglit bank, and crushing and weathering of rock on the left bank
is more predominant. The rock on the right bank is generally sound.



ANNEX 18
Page   2
7.        The ground water table at the damsite is generally low.  Thke table
is higher than the water level of the river at tne rig'nt abutment following
the topographly.  At the left abutment the level more or less coincides with
the river level extending fairly deep into the mountain side.
RE.SERVOIR GEOLOGY
3.        The reservoir leakage problem can be divided into two:  (a) leakage
from the basal rock of the reservoir; and (b) leakage from the dam founda-
tions and the surroundings.
9.        To the west are metamorphic rocks which comprise the backbone of
the Central liountain Range running NWJ-SE at the boundary between the Quae
Yai and Quae Noi basins. To the east, there are distributions of stocks
and batholiths of granite running parallel to the metamorphic rocks. The
strata between these are the Kanchanaburi Series and the Rat Buri limestone
- a semi consolidated Tertiary svstem and a Quarternary system.
10.       The Kanchanabrui Series may be the parent rock of the metamorphiic
rocks. The intrusion of granite is thought to be later than the deposits
of the Rat Buri limestone.  The geological ages are not clearly known, but
it is assumed that the Kanchanaburi Series are of the Carboniferous period
or earlier and the Rat Buri limestone either of the Carboniferous or
Permian period.
11.       Whilst the Kanchanaburi Series are severely folded, the folding
in the overlying Rat Buri limestone is weak. The only fault found is a
thrust fault running along the western slope of the Central Mountain Range.
12.       Core borings were carried out on the flat areas on both banks of
the damsite. It was found that the elevation of the ground water table at
the flat area of the left bank is at an elevation of between 178-209 m above
sea level, sloping gently towards the reservoir in a NW direction. On the
right bank the ground water table varies between 171-181 m parallel with the
topography. It is considered that water collected on the right bank will
flow into the Quae Yai River upstream of the rigl2t abutment. No cavities
were discovered which could serve as passageways for water.
13.       There should be no fear of leakage froma the reservoir because:
(a) the Rat Buri limestone with developed karstic phenomena is
located above reservoir full supply level (180 m.a.s.l.)
(b) strata underlying the limestone are alternations of calcareous
sandstone, impure shaly limestone and shale. ilo karstic
phenomena were found. There is little possibility of caves
being fromed;
(c)  below the strata mentioned in (b) above, quartzite and slate
is found serving as a barrier against leakage on the eastern
side;



ANNEX 18
Page   3
(d)  metamorphic rock to thie west serves as a barrier against
leakage at the xestern side;
(e)  the major trib)utaries flowing into the Quae Yai have continuous
flowing water at points higher than the reservoir high water
level even in the dry season;
(f)  bore holes drilled on the left and right bank showed the ground
water table to be about equal or higher than reservoir full
supply level; on the vast flat area on the left bank the ground
water table slopes to the N, flowing towards the reservoir; and
(g)  preliminary groutino tests indicate that the water tightness
of the dam foundations can be adequately improved.
DAM'ISITE GROLOGY
Lcft Abutment
14.       Below an elevation of about 100 m, the left abutment is covered
by a thin topsoil layer about 2-3 m dleep. At higher elevations topsoil
thickness is substantially less. At the foot of the slope, terrace deposits
do not exceed 10 ma.
15.       3edrock can be divided into two varieties.  One is calcareous
sancdstone in tlle vicinity of the dar axis from elevation 140 mil to the river
bed.  The othler is qtlartzite above elevation 140 r.i at the dan axis.  Tile
boundary between the two is slightlv sheared and crushed, and the rock is
brittle.
16.       The thickness of calcareous sandstone layers is about 30-150 cm
with intercalations of thin layers of slhaly limestone and quartzite. The
character of the rock is hard, but where folding is severe, crushing and
weathering are predominant and the rock is brittle.  Intercalated shale
tias become clay in most cases.
17.       Ouartzite is less crtuslled and weathlered but has thin clay seamas
along, bedding plaaes.  Rock pieces are haard.  Development of cracking is
prominent and browniish weathered surfaces along the cracks continue to fairly
great del)th,s.  Permeability of these parts at hiigh elevations is therefore
estir,ated to be high.
18.       Below elevation 90-100 Ti there is a fairly large-scale weak zone.
Tae widthi at elevation 6( ma is about 130 m along tiie dam axis and is believed
to continiue in the upstreai-doinistream directioni parallel to the river.
TPere is outcropping of sotund bedroclk at the river bed, and in borings to
a cdeptli of 100 m, this weak zone was not found.  In two exploratory adits
(El.( 60.35 m ancl El, 72.32 m) excavated inT this wealk zone, there exist fairly
thick masses of hard rock in irregular forms.

Page   4
19.       The weak zone is seen at a depth of 20 ri from the portal of the
first adit. From this point to 90 m there are repetitions of slightly hard
rock intercalated by thin slheared portions 5-10 m thick and completely crush
weathered zones 3-5 m thick. From 90 m to the face of the adit (at 101.3 m
from the portal) there is a continuation of a completed crushed weathered
zone, although hard quartzite can be found at the bottom part of the facing.
Correlating with a drill hole, it is estimated that the conditions found
in the adit exist over a fairly wide area and deeper into the rock than the
face of the adit.
2').      This weak zone was formed due to the compounding of wieathering
action on the crushed rock following folding. It is expected that the
condition will improve at deeper elevations.  There was no water found at
either of the test adits. Clay is well compacted even in the weak zone.
Preliminary test grouting carried out indicates that cement grouting is
effective to treat this zone.
21.       Besides the weak zone, the left abutment has small-scale faults
at the boundary between calcareous sandstone and quartzite near an eleva-
tion of about 140 m. But there is a wide distribution of hard bedrock above
an elevation of 100 m.  Even at parts of hard rock in the test adits there
are a nwaber of non-continuous small-scale cavities filled with secondary
limae or soil but all are located near the ground surface.
River Bed
22.       Besides small-scale sand and gravel deposits below elevation 65 m
tihere are some sand deposits. However, outcrops of sound quartzite are seen
over wide areas of the river bed in the vicinity of the dam axis. Underlying
these is limestone intercalated with thin layers of shale and calcareous
sandstone. The bedrock at the river bed is generally fresh and sound. There
is no large-scale wealk zone as on the left bank. Cracks are nunerous to a
depth of about 50 m. These are not filled with clay and permeability is
high and the cement grout intake is large.
Rig it Abutment
23.       The rocks on this abutment are calcareous sandstone, quartzite,
shale and limestone. Calcareous sandstone is predominant with other rock
types intercalated in various thiclkness and forms.
24.       Foundation rock is fresh ancd sound and test adits and borings
indficate that the groundwater level is higher than the river water level.
ThIcre are, however, places of severe local folding and there are parts with
crushling and( weathering.  Permeability tests carried out showed favorable
results.
