Page   5
Evaluation of the Bedrock for Dam Foundation
25.       There is a large wieak zone from the lower to middle part of the
left abutment and many cracks in calcareous sandstone and quartzite at higher
elevation. Weathering reaches deep and permeability is high. In the
preparation of the dam foundation, extremely fissured, weathered and soft
portions will be removed and the weaker portions excavated. The foundation
rock in the river bed is hard and has adequate bearing strengtii for a
rockfill dam. This applies also to the right abutment.
26.       The ground water table has been confirmed by a total of 8 drill
holes. On the left abutment the table is low and at about the same level
as the river. On the right abutment the water table is higher and maintains
a level more or less in agreement with the topography. Preliminary grouting
tests were carried out on the dam axis as were permeability tests. These
tests indicated that caution will be required in the preparation of tile
foundation (see paragraphs 27 to 30). Further grouting tests are necessary
in order to determine the most effective and economic way to consolidate
the foundation.
Dam Foundation Treatment Program
27.       Based on present information derived from geological investiga-
tions, permeability tests, ground water measurements, and preliminary
grouting tests, the following paragraphs summarize the proposed foundation
treatment program which, however, is subject to the results of an extensive
grout testing program to be carried out from January-July 1973.
28.       Besides a grout curtain to prevent leakage, blanket grouting is
to be carried out to prevent not only water seepage but alto to improve
the meclhanical properties of the surface layer of the foundations for
the impervious core of the dam. In particular to secure safety against
piping action at the zone of poor geology at the left abutment, a core
cut-off trench will be provided and the deeper parts grouted. For the
lower parts of the right and left bank and the river bed a foundation
gallery is forseen to enable curtain grouting to be carried out and to
serve as a maintenance and inspection gallery. Curtain and blanket grouting
at the right bank, rim areas and in the vicinity of the intake will be
carried out after excavation.
29.       It will, however, be necessary to modify the present plan as
circumstances dictate by the availability of work performance and new geol-
ogical data.
30.       Objectives.  The i'rcatment Porgram has the following objectives:
(a)  Water tightness.  To reduce permeability to 2 Lugeons -/ but
to accept 5 Lugeons when the depth from the foundation surface
is deeper than 50 m or the dam height is less than 30 m.
1/   1 Lugeon is equivalent to a leakage in liters/min from 1 meter length of
drill hole under a pressure of 10 kg/cm



ANNEX 18
Page   6
(b)  Grout Curtain.  Standard depth 50-70 m and width 5-10 m.
Width of blanket grouting will, however, be 10-15 m and
depending on geological conditions will be carried out over
a part or full width of the impervious core foundation.
(c) Grout Strength. Cement content at least 30%.
(d) Spacing of Grout Curtain Rows. Carried out along the dam
axis in one to three rows. At tlhe river bed and left abut-
ment where permeability is high - 3 rows spaced 3.0 n apart
to a depth of 50-60 m from the excavated surface or from the
grouting tunnel.
Left Bank Saddle
31.       At full reservoir level this saddle is about 200-300 n wide and
500 m along.  The elevation of the top of the saddle is more than 50 m
above full reservoir level, but both the upstream and downstream sides have
deeply eroded ravines. The risk of leakage is therefore great and detailed
geological investigations were carried olut. In the upstream ravine, rock
outcrops can be seen from an elevation of 100-380 m. Rock comprises banded
quartzite with a thickness from 20 cm - 100 cm and with an intercalation of
thini beds of calcareous sandstone.  Above 340 m the rock character is fresh,
soun(d, compact and good.
32.       The downstreain ravine is covered with overburden and has less
outcrops.  From available outcrops it can be considered that the geological
features encountered upstream continue downstream with little change. No
larae scale faults pass through the saddle.
33.       Fresh, sound cores were recovered from drill holes.  Permeability
test gave values of only several Lugeons at depths below 40 m. Sheared
zones encountered are on the whole water tight and compact.  The rock of the
saddle is of relative good quality and leakage froia the reservoir would be
insignificant. Nevertheless, adequate foundation treatment is necessary
to prevent a wash-out of materials in sheared zones and cracks.
Forebay
34.       The forebay connecting to the power intake struicture and spillway
is planned at a location fairly distant from the right dam abutment.  The
rock in this area consists of calcareous sandstone and quartzite intercalated
witlh thtin layers of shale.  Weathered rock is to be found to a depth of only
10-29 m and core recovery was about 100%.  The rock is sound and the bedding
planes are parallel to the slope of the mountain. Attention will, however,
need to be given to excavation to ensure stability of the west side. Per-
meability to a depth of 25 m is high but the rock is virtually impermeable
below 80 m.



ANNEX 18
Page   7
Penstock and Spillway
35.       These structures are close to each other.  Test adits and borings
were carried out. The geology is similar to the forebay. However, since
layers of limestone are intercalated it may be possible that cavities are
uncovered during excavation but should not be detrimental to the structures.
The bedrock has adequate bearing strength for the structures and at the
spillway site is of good quality. The bedding planes at the chute portion
of the spillway are parallel to the mountain slope and precautions need to
be taken to ensure slope stability. This also applies to the penstock area.
Powerhouse
36.       The bedrock is chiefly calcareous sandstone with intercalation of
thin layers of slhale and limestone. In an exploratory adit a cave was found
wqith a width of 8 m filled with talus deposits at the ceiling. This cave is
at a depth of less than 10 m from the ground surface and is considered to be
localized formed by erosion of the thin limestone layer. Borings did not
uncover other caves. The bedrock has adequate bearing strength as a founda-
tion for the power house. Care needs to be taken to stabilize excavated
slopes.
Conclusions
37.       Since EGAT's decision to proceed with investigations in 1968, con-
tinuous geological investigations have been carried out. The engineering
geological conditions encountered have been taken into account in the
design of the structures. Nevertheless, in respect to foundation treatment,
civil engineering and engineering geology judgements will have to be made
as new infortation becomes available. EGAT's decision to retain the
service of individual members of the International Consulting Board is
therefore sound and correct.
38.       EPDC's Design Report is based on substantial information such as
27 exploratory adits with a total length of about 1240 m and 45 drill holes
with a total length of about 2740 m. The cost estimate reflects the results
of investigation and includes only one physical uncertainty. This concerns
tlhe extent of the necessary foundation treatment and the amount of cemient
intake needed for grouting. To determine this EGAT has agreed to an exten-
sive grout testing program to be carried out from January - July 1973. Thie
results of thlis program will be communicated to prospective bidders to
enable them to prepare realistic bids through the removal of the present
uncertainties.  Onice informatiora can no longer serve this purpose the
testing program will be ten;iirated.

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTiIORITY OF TIHAILAND)
APPRAISAL QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
ECOLOGICAL RECONNAISSANCE
1.        INTRODUCTION
1.01      At the request of EGAT, an Ecological Reconnaissance for the Quae
Yai Hydro-Electric Project was carried out by a team from the Asia Institute
of Technology, headed by Professor 14. G. McGarry. A report was completed
on November 4, 1972 summarized below: The objectives of the study were to:
(a) identify potential problems caused by the scheme; and
(b) recomnend action to overcome or satisfactorily minimize
identified problems.
1.02      Apart from hiydro power benefits other benefits can be realized by
the construction of the scheme.  They are:  tourism, fisheries, low flow
augmentation for downstream irrigation and navigation, downstream flood
protection, control of saltwater intrusion and pollution, and communications
in the Quae Yai valley.
1.03      Health and welfare aspects of about 8,000 inhabitants whose homes
would be inundated by the reservoir were investigated.  Consideration was
given to their relocation, changes in occuption, life-style, physical and
social surroundings and recommendations were made. Consideration was given
to the biotic communities of the reservoir area, and the value of forestry
stands in the inundated area was assessed. The potential for aquatic plant
growth in the Quae Yai valley was evaluated and counter measures recommended.
1.ii4     Other aspects considered were (a) possibility of incompatibility
of the scheme's power operations witlh other resource needs of the region;
(b) changes in aesthetic quality of the area; (c) downstream scour; (d)
watershed erosion; and (e) the impact of the dam on ground water levels and
physico-chemical changes in agricultural soils of irrigation areas.
1.05      The Quae Yai basin i, viewed as being of prehistoric and historic
archaeological value; several important finds have been made in the region
in recent years.



ANNEX 19.1
Page 2
2.        SUMARY
2.01      The Quae Yai Hydro-Electric Development, as presently foreseen,
could consist of five dams. The ecological reconnaissance report concerns
itself with the lower-most dam at Ban Chao Nen, the object of the present
appraisal. It was found difficult to extend the reconnaissance to the other
4 dam areas because of (a) their inaccessibility; and (b) insecurity in the
upper reaches.
Health
2.02      For the health survey, more than 1,000 people or 1 in 8 were examined.
The major health problem is malaria which, if not combatted, could seriously
affect the health of persons migrating to the dam during its construction
and the inhabitants of the area thereafter. Filarinsis is currently not a
serious hiealth problem although it is likely to be exacerbated during and
stibsequent to dam construction.  Haemorrhagic fever outbreaks are now cormmon
in Tlailand and vectors were found in the area.  Waterborne parastic diseases
are considered not to be a major threat, although suclh diseases as opis-
thorchiasis and paragonimiasis are likely to be introducedl by in-migrating
laborers from N-E Thailand.  'iost other infections diseases experienced in
Thailand could become a problem unless adequate provisions are made and the
necessary measures taken. All of the above-mentioned diseases may be con-
trolled through adequate health services and eradication programs.
Resettlement
2.03      The reservoir will displace about 8,000 inhabitants.  hle majority
of these are Thais, although a large Karen community exists in tae upper
reacihes. Alono the valley, agriculture and the harvesting of bamboo from
thie forest are tihe major occupations. Acquired earnings are utilized to
purchiase food from downstream as insufficient rice and meat are produced
locally to meet the needs of the valley. Coamunication and transport are
largely by boat.  Villages comprise houses of bamboo and the more solid
tinber construction.
2.(4      Villages are made up of clusters of 5 - 3 lhouses belonging to
closely related people and are situated close to the river. Land holdings
vary from 1 - 50 ha, shifting agriculture is used -- with cultivated areas
normLally 1 - 5 ha.  All of the basin was and remains crowni land.  Because
of the lack of any survey, no inhabitant has a title deed to his land.  The
majority have not acquired the right to use the land they do, but have become
wiell established and enjoy free access to unlimited land.
2.0)5     Most of the people are aware of the proposed scheme, are not pleased
with leaving their homes, but face their future in a fatalistic attitude --
particularly the Thais. The Karens on the other hand express a strong desire
not to be resettled downstream and intend to resettle upstream. Both Thais
and 1Karens would prefer their villages and families remaining as physical
and social entities because of kinship and mutual support.



ANNEX 19.1
Page 3
2.06      Two resettlement sites have been proposed -- both are in the
vicinity of the dam and more investigations into these and alternative
sites is required. The present proposal is to provide relocated inhabitants
with personal loans, land, roads, schools, health stations and compensation
grants. Fair distribution of land remains a problem. The report recommends
that the area and site of compensatory land should not be based on land
exploitation rights previously granted by the government. The valley's
population is used to good soil conditions with plentiful water and a source
of cash income from bamboo harvesting. The later would not be available
on the new sites. Resettlement areas should be provided with adequate
drinking and irrigation water sources and good land for at least subsistence
living. Education extension services should be made available to assist
in the transfer from shifting to conventional upland and irrigation agri-
culture or fishing.
Fauna and Forestr
2.07      Wild life would not be seriously affected and there are commer-
cially exploitable stands of forests in the area proposed for inundation.
Fisheries
2.03      A benefit realized by the construction of other reservoir projects
relate to fisheries. A sizable return from fish production may be expected
-- but fish harvest by dynamite must be prohibited. Inhabitants in the
area could benefit if the number of fishermen are maintained at an optimum
level and sound marketing practices are encouraged and adhered to. The
dam's interference of up and downstream fish migration during spawning cycles
is considered not to be significant in view of the prevalence of lateral
migration patterns of local fish.
Aquatic Plants
2.09      These, which may be a potential problem to reservoir management,
were not found upstream during the reconnaissance, nevertheless it is
considered likely that they do exist and that problems, particularly with
water hyacinth, waterfern, and water lettuce, which were all observed down-
stream in the irrigation areas, could arise. Recommendation as to the metnods
of dealing with such problems have been made such as (a) mechanical control
-- usually inefficient but produces less hazards to fish, wildlife etc;
(b) chemical control -- usually easier, faster and longer lasting (most new
herbicides will not harm crops or fish if used correctly); and (c) biological
control -- well developed at laboratory level, but for Thailand would require
research and development. Control is also possible by the introduction of
certain species of herbevorous fislh.



ANNEX 19.1
Page 4
Irrigation
2.10      There is little likelihood of the Hydro Power Project being
incompatible with the use of the water discharged for irrigation purposes.
Benefits would accrue to downstream navigation and irrigation by low flow
augmentation during the dry season. Likewise, downstream flood control
would benefit durtng the %'ct season.  During the first phase period of the
projects operation (3 x 120 IV = 360 1157) no downstream re-regulating pond
would be provided.  (This pond would form part of the second phase which
would also provide for the installation of two additional 180 MW turbine-
pump units). Thus, daily fluctuations of discharge related to power demand
would initially give rise to peak waves immediately downstream of the dam;
navigation would have to be prohibited from this reach.
Arciaeological Aspects
2.11      Important prehistoric artifacts have been found in the Quae Yai
basin area covering the Pleistocene and the Hoabinhian age through early
farming to the beginings of metallurgy. The Ongba cave, located above the
proposed reservoir's water level, has been found rich in artifactual material.
The deposits date from the early Hoabinhinian occupation (135,000 BC).
The first domestication of plants and stone pottery leading to the develop-
ment of pottery took place in Southeast Asia and provided the idea of agri-
culture to the West. Although sites above the reservoir level would not
be inundated, they would be made more accessible to pillage.
Tourism
2.13      A positive benefit likely to result would be related to tourism --
provided the dam is constructed and maintained in such a way as to minimize
aesthetic deterioration of its immediate vicinity. Some problems relate to
the proposed location of open fluorite mining operations, elevated rock
quarries and transmission line routes.
ilining
2.i4      There is some possibility of heavy metal contamination in the
reservoir from upstream lead mines, particularly if proposed expansion
plans materialize. Further investigation is necessary.
3.        RECOMMIMNDATIONS
3.01      A final decision as to the location of resettlement sites has not
yet been made. Only guidelines are available. Investigations should be
carried out on the proposed and alternative sites. Surveys of existing
upstream inhabitants' holdings and properties should be carried out and a
means of compensation established.  Detailed plans should be drawn up on
resettlement sites to include monetary compensation; administration; health



ANNEX 19.1
Page 5
and1 welfare services; and educational and rehabilitation programs. A
coordinating body should be established (EGAT, the Departments of Public
Welfare, Public Health, Credit and Marketing Cooperatives, Fisheries, Forestry,
RID, Land Cooperative, and Land Developmenit Departments) and a Project
itanager given the responsibility of plan implementation.  It is also recom-
menlded that a separate resettleument site (s) be located upstream for the
Karen people.
3.02      A lhealthl center should be established at the da3asite to be staffed
by a qualified doctor and available to the resettled people and construction
laborers.  Secondary health centers should be located in all sizable resettle-
ment sites. Eradicationi program against malaria, hiaemorrhagic fever and
filariasis should he initiated and expanded. Further investigation is
required to establish wiether the intermediate hosts of waterborne parasitic
diseases are present in the river basin. If found, surveillance of inhab-
itants should be conducted and the epidemaiology of these diseases investi-
gated. The introduction of diseases such as opistorchiasis by way of in-
migrating laborers from other parts of Thailand should be monitored and
preventitive measures taken.
3.03      The consultants recommend that as sooll as possible a dry season
archaeological survey (six montlhs) should be conducted in the watershed.
All valuable artifacts in danger of inundation or made accessible to the
public, should be placed under the protection of the National Museum.
3.04      The benefits associated T,ith fisheries should be realized through
sound management practices. A Department of Fisheries Station should be
located on the reservoir to record catches, control methods of harvest and
cooperate with the Department of Credit and M4arketing Cooperatives in
establishing fish marketing cooperative.
3.05      Aquatic plants are recognized as a potential problem on the
reservoir and in the downstream irrigation system. Provision should be
made for their control.
3.06      Erosion is not considered a threat in terms of reservoir siltation.
However, erosion of topsoil shiould be prevented (construction metlhods and
the practice of shifting agriculture). It is recommended that commercial
exploitation is made of forest stands which are to be inundated and that
timber companies be encouraged to remove all forest stands.
3.07      Fuirther investigations are recommenided into heavy metal contamina-
tion of thie reservoir from lead and zinc miiiing operations.  Iluge quantities
of flourite are to be removed From the vicinity of the damn prior to its
construction.



ANNEX 19.1
Page 6
3.08      If the multi-purpose nature of the dam is to include benefits
through tourism, lowering of tihe high aesthetic quality of the valley should
he avoided. This refers particularly to open mining of flourite, rock
quarries and transmission line locations.
4.        CONCLUSIONS
4.01      Provided the above listed recommendations are implemented, supple-
nentary surveys made and analysed, the consultants -- Asia Institute of
Technology, Prof. Xl. G. McGarry -- are of the opinion that there is no single
ecological consequence or combination of consequences which is of sufficient
importance to deter or preclude construction of the Ouae Yai No. 1 Project.

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
APPRAISAL OF THE
QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
SELECTED ECOLOGICAL SURVEYS
1.        Following the recommendations of the "Ecological Reconnaissance
Report" of November 1972 described in Annex 19.1 EGAT entrusted the Asia
Institute of Technology to carry out selected ecological surveys. A report
dated January 20, 1974 covered the following fields:
(a) Dry Season Water-Borne Parasitic Diseases;
(b) Heavy Metal Pollution;
(c) Aesthetic Qualities of the Quae Yai Basin; and
(d) Archaelogical surveys of the Quae Yai Basin.
The results of these surveys are summarized in the following sections:
Dry Season Water-Borne Parasitic Diseases
2.        Aquatic fauna having potential of being intermediate hosts for
water-borne parasites were investigated and some domestic and wild animals
thought to be reservoir hosts of such parasites were included. Swamps,
ponds and other water bodies of the basin were considered. The parasites
which were of major importance to this investigation were:
(a) Paragonimus - lung fluke (worm)
(b) Opistorchiasis - liver fluke
(c) Schistosoma - blood fluke
(d) Diphyllobotrium - fish tape work
(e) the filaria Brugia; and
(f) Angiostrongylus - the lung worm
Emphasis was laid on the parasite species which were parasitic in man
although animal parasitic species were also considered.



ANNEX 19.2
Page     2
3.        Neither the parasite itself nor its intermediate hosts of human
schistosomiasis were found above or below the proposed dam site. It was
considered unlikely that schistosamiasis will be a problem north of Ban Lad
Ya in the future (about 50 km below the damsite). However, the possibility
of intermediate hosts inhabiting the Quae Yai basin cannot be ruled out in
view of the geographical and climatological similarities between the Quae
Yai River including its downstream irrigation area and other areas of
Thailand known to be inhabited by schistosoma japonicum.
4.        The liver fluke which is endemic amongst the people of Northeast
Thailand was investigated along the Quae Yai through its intermediate
snail and fish hosts. The snail host was found only in the downstream
irrigation area, but the fish was available all along the Quae Yai. No
metacercariae of the liver fluke were found in fish samples taken. Again,
there is a possibility that the liver fluke will inhabit the Quae Yai basin
in the future. It is likely to be brought there by in-migrating labor from
Northleast Thailand during dam construction.
5.        Both the snail and crab intermediate hosts of the lung fluke
were observed in the Quae Yai valley. The stage is set for its import by
in-migrating labor from central Thailand.
6.        The fish tape worm was observed and is also considered likely
to proliferate consequent to dam construction.
7.        Other water-borne parasites such as Gnathostoma and Angiostrongylus
cantonensis were not observed.
Measures to be taken
8.        A detailed survey of such water borne diseases as schistosomiasis,
paragonimiasis and opistorchiasis of the downstream irrigation area is
required. Although the parasitic agents of these diseases were not found,
several of the intermediate hosts have been detected. AIT recommends that
such a survey be carried out by the Faculty of Tropical Medicine, Mahidol
University. In conjunction with the survey AIT recommends:
(a) the maintenance of a medical surveillance program during
and after dam construction. Emphasis on in-aigrating labor
and health education; and
(b) medical surveys during and after construction to cover
all important diseases in order to quantify the actual
health impact of dam construction. Such information would
prove invaluable to future water resource development
projects (Mekong).



ANNEX 19.2
Page     3
Heavy Metal Pollution
9.        Heavy metal pollution of water, soil and aquatic fauna resulting
from upstream lead mining and processing was investigated. With the
possible exception of cadium, the levels of heavy metals in the soil are
not considered to be of medical significance giving rise to concern for
public health. Lead contents of the Quae Yai waters were well within the
drinking water limits suggested by WHO, although somewhat higher than levels
considered to have no significant effect on fish, and higher than background
unpolluted levels of natural waters of Thailand and elsewhere. Cadium, zinc
and copper levels were so low as not to give rise to concern for public health.
10.       The p. H., hardness and quiescent characteristics of the proposed
reservoir could well serve to reduce present levels of lead in the Quae
Yai's waters.
Measures recommended
11.       A momitoring program for heavy metals and fluorides should be
initiated at the time of first impoundment of the Quae Yai waters. Specific
emphasis should be laid on heavy metals in the waters of the Huai Khitti
and Huai Dika/Klu, tributaries of the Quae Yai. Waste waters of the Khitti
flotation plant should be treated. A health survey of the Bo Ngam village
should be conducted. If as suspected, either plumbism or the Itai - Itai
disease is evident, then the laborers' village should be moved to an
unpolluted site or the slag washing operation stopped. EGAT should take
steps to ensure the protection of the reservoir from heavy metal pollution.
Pollution control may be effected either by adequate treatment of wastewater
streams or by restriction of mining activities.
Aesthetic Qualities of the Quae Yai Basin
12.       A positive benefit is likely to result as a consequence of the dam
through improved access to the Quae Yai River if the dam is constructed
and maintained to minimize deterioration of the aesthetic qualities of its
surrounds. The Quae Yai is crossed by the famous "bridge on the river Kwai"
and has within its basin historic and prehistoric sites of archaelogical
importance, caves and the very beautiful and popular Erawan Falls.
13.       Problem areas are:
(a) quarry sites for rock-fill material and
soil borrow pits for the earth core;
(b) transmission line route; and
(c) flourite mining operations.



ANNEX 19.2
Page     4
Recommendations
14.       Alternative upstream quarry sites which will eventually be
inundated by the reservoir should be sought after. If these cannot be
found because of the necessary quality, the downstream rock quarry proposed
should be quarried in terraces upon which top-soil should be placed fol-
lowed by reforestation. Likewise the soil borrow areas should be reforested
after soil removal. A transmission line route has been recommended which
would be environmentally and technically acceptable. Increased costs over
other alternatives would be small. Flourite mining operations six kilo-
meters northwest of the dam will leave scars on the hill sides adjacent to
the reservoir. Steps should be taken to return this area as close as
possible to its original condition.
Archaeological Studies
15.       Studies have demonstrated the presence of several archaeological
sites which would be inundated or provided with access resulting in pillage.
The authors of the report recommend further investigations, mapping and
where necessary evacuation of artifacts. It is further recommended that
the National Museum of Fine Arts and/or the Faculty of Archaeology, Silpakorn
University be contacted to implement the recommendations.
Summary
The authors conclusions are summaried as follows:
"Reference is made to the former ecological reconnaissance's several
recommendations not covered by investigations reported upon above, these
recommendations still stand. Apart from these and provided that the above
recommendations are implemented, it is considered that there is no single
ecological consequence or combination of consequences which is of sufficient
importance to deter or preclude construction of the Ban Chao Nen Dam".

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
OUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT
PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
AID
ECONOMIC A4ALYSIS
TIIE POWER MARKET
1.       T-he project is one component of EGAT's 1973-1982 generation and
transmission program designed to meet the forecast demand of power and
energy. In 1962, EGAT, respectively its predecessors, was serving a maximum
demiand of _13 11W and was generating 512 MvWh at an annual average load factor
of 51.6%. At the end of 1972, ten years later, maximum demand served by
ECAT had increased to 1030 MW, generation had increased to 5711 GWh, and
the average annual load factor had improved to 63.3%. In a ten year period
therefore, theire was a ten-fold increase in power and energy requirements.
2.        The average annual growth rates for the 1962-1972 period were:
abouit 25% for maximum demand; and about 27% for generatioin.  Growth was not
conistant over the period but varied on an annual basis betweeni 35.9% in
1965/66 and 15%' in 1970/71 for maximum demand; and between 40.6%yo in 1964/65
an(I 17/2% in 1970/71 for generation. The growth of power and energy require-
ments correlated closely with general econotic development.  Growth stiinu-
lants were rapidly rising investments, favorable world rice mlarkets, and
exports of services.  The turniiig point in development came in 1969, due to
a combination in a decline in US military expenditures and transfers, and
a reduction in the prices of rtbber and rice. The growth rate of maximum
demaind served by EGA, fell from 24.1% in 196O/69 to 15% in 1970/71 and in
generation from 27.2% in 1963/69 to 17.2% in 1970/71.  rhe 1971/72 growth
rates, however, showed a recovery to 19.9% for maximum demand and 19.3% for
generation. The reversal of the 1969-71 trend in 1972 is due in part to
vigorous entrepreneurship and adaptable labor.
3.        For Thailand as.a whole, electrical energy generated per capita
rose from 85 IdWh in 1968 to 153 kWh in 1972, at an average annual growth rate
of 15.8%. The total number of electricity consumers increased during the
same period from 694,514 to 967,462 at an average annual growth rate of 8.8%
and generation per consumer increased from 3990 kWh to 6030 kWhl at an average



Ai4NEX 20.1
Page 2
annual growthl rate of 11%. Inspite of these substantial growth rates achieved
in the past only an estimated 17% of the total population of Thailand pre-
sently enjoys the benefits of electricity. Therefore, a substantial latent
demand remains to be served.
4.        In 1968 EGAT was generating 88.6% of all electricity consumed in
Thailand (PEA generated 7.3%; and private generating plants 4.1%). At the
end of 1972, EGAT's share had increased to 92.2%, whilst PEA's share dropped
to 3.47 and private generation increased slightly to 4.4%.  Of all power
sold in 1972 by EGAT (direct sales to industries); MEA; and PEA; residential
sales accounted for 20.4%; industrial and commercial sales for 78.8%; and
street lighting for 0.8%. Residential consumption in the Bangkok metropolitan
area served by 1EA in 1968 amounted to about 1490 kWh/residential consumer
and in 1972 to 2020 kWh, an average annual increase of 8%. In the rest of
the country served by PEA, residential consumption in 1968 amounted to 464
kWhl/consumer and in 1972 to 735 kWh, an average annual increase of 12.1%.
The difference between metropolitan residential consumption and the corre-
sponding consumption in the rest of the country, reflects in part the
purchasing power of the individual but the higher growth rate outside the
metropolitan area also reflects the inprovement of conditions in the pro-
vinces, a factor supporting PEA's load forecast.
5.        The Thai Authorities have given considerable attention to rural
electrification. As of December 1970 of the about 45,000 villages in
Thailand, only about 10% were provided with electricity. Village formation
hlas grown at about 1%. per annum so that twentyfive years from now about
54,000 villages would remain for electrification.  To carry out such a
program within 25 years would require yearly investments in excess of US$25.0
million, substantially beyond the plhysical and financial capability of PEA
to carry out. An initial program was therefore prepared which forsees the
electrification of 500 villages/year for the next five years. Incremental
energy and power demand resulting from such a project is modest, totalling
at the end of the fifth year 25 GWh and about 15 {W. This estimate is
based on electricity consumption per consumer per month of 10 kWh in the
first year of connection, and a coincident demand of about 100 W. By the
end of thie 25th year a rural consumer is estimated to require only 70 kWh/
month at a coincident demand of 300 W.  Thus the effect of such a program
oTI EGAT wrill be small.
6.        By 1982 Thailand's total electrical power and energy requirements
are estimated at about 3390 tW and 19700 GWh respectively.  Of this total
EGAT is estimated to provide for 3260 1111 and 19250 GWh. Thus in 1982 EGAT
will provide for about 96% of all demand and 97.7% of all electrical energy.
The average annual growth rates forecast for 1972-1982 are 12%o for demand
and 13% for energy compared to growth rates achieved 1962-1972 of 25% and
27% respectively. These forecast growth rates are not umreasonable reflect-
ing the substantially larger base. In fact, whilst during 1962-1972
maximum demand increased on average at about 92 MI/year, the increase 197 2-
1982 is about 220 MW/year.



ANNEX 290.1
Page   3
7.        As mentioned in paragraph 1, the Quac Yai Project constitutes only
one element of EGAT's generation expansion program.  With an installed
capacity in service of 1423 14W, 1024 MW are presently under construction
and are scheduled to be commissioned between now and late 1975. Of this
incremental capacity 600 MI is thermal (IBPR  financed) and 424 MJ hlydro
(IBRD financed 250 14W).  Between early 1976 and end 1978 a further 502 94W
are scheduled for commissioning and with Quae Yai (360 MA) in service at
the end of 1979, available capacity would total 3367.3 MW of which 3260.8 il
are dependable. 50 M is attributable to increased Nam Ngum imports from
Laos. Pages 1 - 6 of Annex 20.2 provide further data on past and future
development of the power market.
CAPABILITY OF QITAE YAI
8.        Quae Yai will be operated witn priority given irrigation require-
ments. An analysis was therefore necessary to determine whether or not a
conflict exists between irrigation and power. As a first step an irrigation
project was developed, primarily resulting in an up-dating of the 1968 RID
Mieklong Irrigation Project Report. The up-dated project is described in
Annex 1 and the operational studies are described in Annex 17. The analysis
carried out showed that there is no serious conflict between power and
irrigation requirements mainly because EGAT's system - a mixed thermal-hydro
system - is very flexible.  In the wet season, when irrigation requirements
are low (Quae Noi and other downstream tributaries provide sufficient water
to meet irrigation needs), the Quae Yai Project will be operated as a peaking-
plant, thus conserving water1 or dry season needs.  In the dry season Quae
Yai would operate as a base - load plant.
ALTERNATIVES TO THE PROJECT
9.        The alternative to Quae Yai would be a thermal development or a
different ,iydro facility providing the same service. In each case, however,
an irrigation dam would be required at or close to the proposed site to
enable inmnlementation of the proposed irrigation development. In such an
event, full dam costs wqould have to be carried by the irrigation develop-
ment, wBhich then would compete with similar developments in other parts of
Thailand. Since no other hydro developments of similar size are ready
for implementation, the only alternative to Quae Yai is a thermal plant
located at or close to the present load center - Bangkok.
1J.       The proposed project was therefore compared to a thermal alterna-
tive uising residual lheavy oil hs fuel.  Thle comparison was carried out over
a period of 50 years, representing the average life of the hydro facility.
The main variables affecting the comparison are the forecast load growth,
the capital costs of each alternative, the price of residual fuel, and the
capital costs and potential benefits of the irrigation development.
1/   This definition refers to plant utilization factors during the respective
periods.



ANNEX 20.1
Page   4
LOAD GROWTH
11.       Since Quae Yai constitutes only one element of EGAT's generation
expansion program, the assumption of different load growths would have no
direct influence on the decision to proceed with the project. Because the
construction period is 7 years, any ch.mge in the load forecast could be
accommodated by accelerating or deferring projects of a shorter construction
period. The energy available from Quae Yai can be econoaically utilized
Anitain EGAT's system independently of load forecast variations normally
assumed, as its output in 1982 would represent only 8.5% of dependable
capacity and only 6.5% of energy demand forecast for that year.
CAPITAL COSTS
12.       Three different values were used for hydro and thermal station
costs.
(a) Thermal power plant. Essentially these are based on the costs
of the Bangkok No. 4 unit. Since this unit is an extension of
an existing power plant, an alloT.ance was also made for site
development costs. Because of increased auxiliary requirements
of a thermal station as compared to a hydro station, off-set
in part by reduced transmission losses, the equivalent thermal
capacity was estimated at 380 MW. Thus the estimated range of
costs with their probabilities are:
(i) M  1450 millions (US$190/kW); probability 0.15
(ii) g 1600 millions (US$210/kW); probability 0.65
(iii) $ 1750 millions (US$230/kW); probability 0.20
The range adopted is equal to $ 300 million or about + 9.4%.
(b) Quae Yai costs. Total costs of Quae Yai (excluding interest
during construction and price contingencies) are estimated
at $ 2684.5 million. This estimate, is considered the base
estimate and a probability of 0.7 was assigned.  A high
estimate of $ 3,000 million was assumed and a low estimate
of $ 2,370 million. Both high and low estimates were
assigned an equal probability of 0.15 each. Thus the range
taken is equal to $ 630 million or aibout + 11.7%.

FUEL PRICES
15.       Fuel costs were anlaysed by two independent methods:
(a) based on past fuel contracts; and
(b)  based on world market trends.
The results can be summarized as follows:
EGAT purchases its fuel from a Government-owned refinery and
signs three year contracts. The basic price paid by EGAT remains virtually
constant in a three year period as increases in refinery prices are off-set
by decreases in taxes. The ex-refinery price before tax etc. is presently
$ 0.2814 per liter or about 35.6 US i/million BTU. By 1979 it was calculated
(based on past trends) that the ex-refinery price before tax etc. would
increase to $ 0.455 per liter or about 57.5 US U/million BTU. Thus the
average amnual increase would amount to 8.2%. In comparison Middle East
Crude Oil prices are estimated to increase in-the period 1970-1975 at 8.5%
p. a.

17.       Further fuel cost increases as from 1979 have been allowed for to

of exploration, development and shipbuilding costs, etc. The annual growth
rate has been assumed at 5% which is not unreasonable. In addition the
calculated fuel costs were increased by 2% to reflect lube-oil requirements.

BTU/kWh over the useful life of the unit.  (A very favorable assumption)
IRRIGATION1 VEVFLOPiMENT
19.       The assumptions made to evaluate the irrigation project's economic
justification are given in Annex 1.  These may be summarized as follows:
(a) Benefits. They have been assumed to be increases in agri-
cultural production only and natural improvements have been
disregarded since it was assumed that they would be of the
same magnitude for the without-project case as for the with-
project case. Incremental benefits have been estimated by
deducting a 50-year stream of present condition benefits
from the 50-year stream of estimated benefits. Prices used
are estimated farm - or factory gate prices. In most crops
these prices exclude net benefits accruing to the economy
through the marketing process, namely taxes, rents, profits,
etc., thus lowering future total benefits. In the case of
rice, however, benefits accruing to the Government through
Export Premiums and Taxes were added. Dry season benefits
have been assumed to accrue one year after completion of
on-farm works, but existing sugar cane areas were assumed
to suffer a loss of half their annual benefit during on-farm
construction. The development period assumed to reach full
production once on-farm works were completed and supporting
services available, was 5-6 years.
(b) Capital Costs. Past investments in Stage I area and in the
diversion barrage have been treated as sunk costs. Incre-
mental capital costs are summarized as follows:
Stage I Area             8 .388.8 million
Stage II Area        f 2,710.8 million
Total                $ 3,599.8 million or US$173.0 million
The construction period of Stage I would be the years
1973-1984 and for Stage II, 1973-1978 and 1983-1995.
Progress rates would average (based on dry season
irrigated area) 1400 ha/annum in the Stage I area
and 18,000 ha/annum in the Stage II area.



ANNEX 20.1
Page 7
(c) Operation and Maintenance Costs. These would increase
from $ 22.3 million in 1973 to $ 100.2 million in 1995.
For the Stage I distribution system they have been assumed
to commence immediately, while for other area the assump-
tion was made that they would start one year before the
specific distribution systen has been fully developed.
Operation and maintenance of on-farm works were assumed to
begin on completion of these works. Supporting services
were assumed to be established in units serving 50,000 ha.
These units have been assumed to commence operations, and
thus costs, upon full development of any part of a unit.
COST ALLOCATION
20.       The principle of cost allocation between the Royal Irrigation
Department (RID) and EGAT was approved by the Government in March 1970.
The metlhod used to allocate costs was approved by the Co-mittee of Water
Resources in April 1972. This method, the "Separable Costs Remaining
Benefits liethod" is acceptable and resulted in the Government's agreement
to provide EGAT with $ 600 million towards the construction of the project.
RESULTS OF THE ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
21.       To analyze the economic merits of the proposed project, three
approaches were taken:
(a)  comparison of the proposed project with an oil-
fired steam alternative providing the same
service, with the project bearing:
(i) all costs; and
(ii) all costs less $ 600 million allocated to
irrigation.
The comparison was based on the discounted cash flows of the
two alternatives over a period of 50 years. To allow for
uncertainties, a probability analysis was used to establish
the mean value and range variation of the discount rate which
equalized the present wvorth of the two alternatives, assuming
three different values, with corresponding probabilities
assigned to them, for each of the main variables (capital costs
and fuel costs);
(b) determination of the equalized discount rate of a cost
and benefit stream over 50 years of the Identified
irrigation project downstream of Quae Yai. In carrying
out this analysis it was assumed that:



ANNEX 20.1
Page   8
(i) no Quae Yai costs would be allocated to
irrigation; and
(ii) $ 600 million of Quae Yai costs are
allocated to irrigation; and
(c)  comparison of the proposed project and a downstream
irrigation project with an alternative development
stream containing oil fired steam generation but no
irrigation. Total investment costs, including
operating and maintenance costs of the multipurpose
project were reduced by the incremental benefits
attributable to irrigation, thus giving a net
multipurpose project cost stream. This analysis
was carried out over a period of 50 years.

ANNEX 24

THAILAND
ELECTRICITY GENERATING AUTHORITY OF THAILAND
BAN CHAO NEN HYDRO-ELECTRIC PREDJECT
(QUAE YAI HYDRO-ELECTRIC PROJECT)
Assumptions in Financial Forecasts
1. *REVENUE
A tariff increase of 4-1/2% on average (1.3 stang) has been
assumed from FY 1975 onwards.
2.        FUEL PRICE
EGAT's fuel price at July 1, 1973 was made up of:
Basic price          28.14 stang/liter - 35.19 US4/1 mill Btu
Excise tax            4.00 stang/liter
Business tax          0.68 stang/liter
Rent for Government-
owned Refinery      7.00 stang/liter
39.82 stang/liter = 49.8 US4/1 mill Btu
This price has been assumed for calculation of fuel costs throughout the
period of the forecasts.
3.        FUEL SURCHARGE
No fuel surcharge has been assumed in the forecasts on the basis
that any increase in the cost of fuel above that prevailing in July 1973
will be offset by the tariff increase to cover fuel cost increases and the
fuel cost adjustment clause.
4.        PURCHASED POWER
With the completion of the Nam Ngum project in Laos (fiscal
year 1972), EGAT plans to import between 230 GWh and 420 GWh per year during
the period fiscal 1974 through 1982. According to EGAT, the price for energy
has been fixed at $ 0.09/kwh; this price, therefore, has been included in
the financial projections.



ANNEX 24
Page   2
5.        OPERATION, MAINTENANCE AND ADMINISTRATION
The forecast of operation and maintenance expenses is based on
a detailed analysis carried out by EGlT of the number and qualificatio*
of staff employed at the end of fiscal year 1972 and of the level of
expenditures other than salaries and wages by each physical facility
(e.g. each generating unit) throughout Thailand. Administration expense
is increased by 10% a year from 1974.
6.        DEPRECIATION
EGAT's depreciation is based on the straight-line method. The
rates were revised in October 1971 to reflect operational experience of
EGAT. The more significant old and new rates are as follows:
Annual Depreciation Rate
Old                  New
Thermal Plants
(Oil and lignite)
Boiler and Accessories                 3%                    4%
Turbines and Generators
including Accessories                3%                    4%
Structures                             3Z (Oil)              4Z
4% (Lignite)
Thermal Plants
(Diesel)
Structures, Prime Movers,
Accessory Equipment                  7.5%                  7.5%
Hydro Plants
Reservoir, Dam, Spillway
and Outlets                      1.25% - 1.33%         1.25% - 1.33%
Turbines and Generators
including Accessories                3%                    441-
Structures                             3%                    4%
Transmission System
Towers,                                2.5%                  2.5%
Poles and Fixtures                   3% - 20%              4% - 20%
Structures                             3Z                    4X
/1   The forecast income statement reflects depreciation from the
estimated month of operation of various plants.



ANNEX 24
Page   3
7.        INCOME TAX
According to their Acts, EGAT and its predecessors were
required to turn over their total net profits to the Government. Since
none of the entities in fact financially were in a position to live up
to this stipulation, the Government decreed in 1965 that all State
Enterprises should pay instead the equivalent of the corporate income
tax which amounts to about 25% of the net profit. Even this tax, however,
was not paid, and eventually accumulated to $ 204,8 million in EGAT's
books by the end of fiscal year 1969. During negotiations for Loan 655-TH,
the Government agreed to convert the deferred tax amount into a 6%,
15-year loan amortization starting in fiscal year 1973 and to exempt
EGAT from paying income tax to the Government during the period 1970-1976
unless its debt/equity ratio fell below 1:1.2 (i.e. total debt outstanding
would become less than 45.5% of the total capitalization). At the same
time, the Government committed itself to review EGAT's income tax situa-
tion and arrive at a permanent solution before the end of fiscal 1976.
For the reasons explained in the report, the financial forecast assumes
that EGAT w'.ll continue to be exempted from income tax throughout the
1974-82 period.
8.        FORECAST EXTERNAL SOURCES
EGAT's future needs for external funds are estimated to be
obtained as follows:
(a) the proposed IBRD loan of US$75.0 million
equivalent assumed to be at 7.25% for a term
of 26 years including 6 years of grace;
(b)  the proposed OECF loan of US$41.0 million
equivalent assumed to be at 3.25% for a term
of 25 years including 7 years of grace;
(c) agreed loans from OECF for six projects aggre-
gating to US$90.0 million equivalent assumed to
be at 3.25% and 4.5% and for terms ranging from
25 years including 7 years grace to 20 years
including 5 years of grace;
(d) agreed loans from ADB for four projects aggre-
gating to US$65.0 million equivalent assumed to
be at 7.5% and for terms of 22 and 20 years with
4 and 5 years of grace;
(e) small balances to be drawn on suppliers' credits
from Austrian Banks at 4.5% for a term of 20 years
including 5 years of grace;



ANNEX 24
(f) assumed foreign currency loans for: three
nuclear projects, two hydro projects, exten-
sions to existing plant and transmission
expansion on the following terms: IBRD
(7-1/4%, 25 years including 5 years grace),
Export/Import Bank (7-1/2%, 26 years includ-
ing 6 years of grace), ADB (7-1/2%, 25 years
including 5 years of grace), and OECF (3-1/4%,
27 years including 7 years of grace);
(g)  a series of annual loans partially committed
from Government against project expenditure and
import duties. These are assumed to be at 6%
with repayment periods varying up to 26 years
including 6 years of grace;
(h) Government contributions of $ 1,922.0 million
including $ 600 million for the Ban Chao Nen
Project; and
(i) issue of EGAT Bonds of $ 300 million in 1974 at
8.5% for a term of 5 years.
